والحد السابق هو أحد المبادئ العشرة المنظومة في قول بعضهم (۸) : [من الرجز] إِنَّ مَبَادِي كُلِّ فَنْ عَشَرَهُ الحَدُّ وَالمَوْضُوعُ ثُمَّ الثَّمَرَهُ وَفَضْلُهُ وَنِسْبَةٌ وَالوَاضِعْ وَالاسْمُ الاسْتِمْدَادُ حُكْمُ الشَّارِعُ مَسَائِلٌ، وَالبَعْضُ بِالبَعْضِ اكْتَفَى وَمَنْ دَرَى الجَمِيعَ حَازَ الشَّرَفا فحد هذا الفنّ لغة وشرعاً ما تقدّم. وموضوعُهُ : ذاتُ اللهِ من حيث ما يجب له وما يستحيل وما يجوز، وذاتُ الرُّسل كذلك، والممكنُ من حيث إنه يُتوصل به إلى وجود صانعه (1)، والسمعيات من حيث اعتقادها. وثمرته : معرفةُ الله بالبراهين القطعية، والفوز بالسعادة الأبدية. وفضله : أنه أشرفُ العلوم ؛ لكونه متعلقاً بذاته تعالى وذاتِ رسله وما يتبع ذلك، والمتعلّق - بكسر اللام - يشرف بشرف المتعلَّق - بفتحها - (٢) . ونسبته : أنه أصلُ العلوم الدينيَّة (۳) وما سواه فرغ وما سواه فرح عنه، وما أحسن قول القائل : [من الخفيف] أَيُّهَا المُقْتَدِي (٤) لِتَطْلُبَ عِلْماً كُلُّ عِلْمٍ عَبْدٌ لِعِلْمِ الكَلَامِ (۵) تَطْلُبُ الفِقْهَ كَي تُصَحِّحَ حُكْماً ثُمَّ أَغْفَلْتَ مُنْزِلَ الأَحْكَامِ وواضعه أبو الحسن الأشعري ومَنْ تبعَهُ، وأبو منصور الماتريدي ومَنْ تبعه؛ بمعنى: أنهم دوّنوا كتبه وردُّوا الشُّبة التي أوردتها المعتزلة (٦)، وإلا فالتوحيد جاءَ به كل نبي من لدن آدم إلى يوم القيامة.
And the previous definition is one of the ten principles versified in the saying of some: [in rajaz] Indeed the principles of every art are ten: the definition, the subject, then the fruit, its virtue, its relation, the founder, the name, the derivation, the ruling of the legislator, [and] the issues. And some suffice with some of them, and whoever knows all of them has attained honor. So the definition of this art, linguistically and legally, is what preceded. Its subject is: the essence of God in terms of what is necessary for Him, what is impossible for Him, and what is permissible for Him; and similarly the essence of the messengers; and the contingent being insofar as it leads to the existence of its creator; and the transmitted matters (sam'iyyat) in terms of believing in them. Its fruit is: knowledge of God by definitive proofs and attainment of eternal happiness. Its virtue is that it is the noblest of sciences because it is related (muta'alliq) to His essence, may He be exalted, and the essence of His messengers and what follows that; and the related thing (al-muta'alliq) is honored by the honor of that to which it is related (al-muta'allaq). Its relation is that it is the root of the religious sciences, and everything else is a branch thereof, and it is independent of them. And how excellent is the saying of the poet: [in khafif] O you who seek knowledge, every science is a servant to the science of kalam; you seek fiqh to correct a ruling, then you ignore the Revealer of rulings. Its founder is Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and those who followed him, and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and those who followed him; meaning that they compiled its books and refuted the doubts presented by the Mu'tazila; otherwise, tawhid was brought by every prophet from Adam until the Day of Resurrection.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage explains the ten principles (mabadi) of any science and applies them to ilm al-kalam. The author first quotes a poem listing the ten principles: definition, subject, fruit, virtue, relation, founder, name, derivation, ruling of the legislator, and issues. Then he applies each to kalam: (1) the definition is the legal definition of tawhid given earlier. (2) The subject includes God's essence (with its necessary, impossible, and contingent aspects), similarly the prophets, the possible leading to the Creator, and scriptural matters (sam'iyyat). (3) The fruit is certain knowledge of God and eternal bliss. (4) The virtue is that it is the noblest science because its subject is the noblest (God and His messengers). (5) The relation is that kalam is the root of all religious sciences; all others are branches dependent on it. He quotes a couplet reinforcing that kalam is the master science. (6) The founder: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and their followers who codified the science and refuted the Mu'tazila, though the essence of tawhid was taught by all prophets from Adam onward.

أبو منصور الماتريدي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Imam al-Ash'ari and Imam al-Maturidi invented the concept of tawhid or started a new religion.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that they only compiled and defended kalam, while tawhid itself was brought by every prophet from Adam onward.

Checkpoint
What role do Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi play in relation to ilm al-kalam, according to the passage?
A) They invented the doctrine of tawhid for the first time
B) They compiled its books and refuted the doubts of the Mu'tazila
C) They were the first prophets to preach tawhid
D) They rejected all earlier prophetic teachings

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari and Maturidi organized and defended kalam, but the core of tawhid is the eternal message of all prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If tawhid is the very message that every prophet from Adam to Muhammad devoted their lives to, why do you often treat it as just one subject among many? What would change if you truly believed it is the most urgent truth you will ever encounter?
?
The founders of kalam spent their lives defending the creed against doubts. What doubts about Allah or His attributes do you still allow to linger in your heart without actively seeking to uproot them? Will you today resolve to study and refute at least one such doubt?
?
You have read that the codification of kalam was a defense of prophetic truth, not a new invention. Yet how often do you dismiss theological study as a later ‘innovation’ and neglect it? How can you honour the prophetic legacy by engaging deeply with the very discipline that protects it?

Refuting the Claim that Kalam is a Bid'a
Ibn Hajar al-Haytami refutes the claim that kalam is a bid'a by listing salaf who practiced it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Hajar al-Haytami refutes the accusation that kalam is a religious innovation by showing that the salaf themselves engaged in it and that the prohibition was only against heretical theology.

Core Idea
The salaf engaged in kalam; the prohibition targets heretical theology, not orthodox defense.

Ibn Hajar al-Haytami directly addresses a common criticism of kalam: that it was a blameworthy innovation avoided by the early Muslims. In this passage, he counters by listing prominent Companions and successors who studied and wrote on theological topics. He also clarifies that the changes in kalam after the salaf were only terminological and that the reported prohibitions were directed at the heretical schools of the Jahmiyya and Qadariyya.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Try to grasp the overall argument before analysing details.
2
Identify the list of salaf
Find the names of Companions and successors that Ibn Hajar mentions as having engaged in kalam. Why is this list significant?
The list includes 'Umar, 'Ali, Ibn 'Abbas, etc.
3
Analyze the role of al-Ash'ari
Ibn Hajar explains that kalam is attributed to al-Ash'ari not because he invented it but because he systematized it. Note this distinction.
Al-Ash'ari translated the methods of earlier scholars.
4
Understand the nature of changes
The passage says only titles and terminologies were introduced after the salaf. Compare this to other sciences where similar developments occurred.
5
Distinguish the prohibition
Ibn Hajar concludes by stating that the prohibition was against the Jahmiyya and Qadariyya, not against orthodox kalam. Reflect on how this answers the accusation.
This is the key refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال العلامة المحقق ابن حجر الهيتمي في فتاواه الحديثية ص (۲۰۸) عن علم الكلام: (وما قيل : إنه بدعةٌ لأنه لم ينظر فيه السلف، مع أنه يُورث المراء والجدال والشبهات .. رُدَّ بأنه نظر فيه السلف قطعاً، منهم عمر وابنه وعلي وابن عباس رضي الله عنهم، ومن التابعين عمر بن عبد العزيز وربيعة وابن هرمز ومالك والشافعي رضي الله عنهم، وألف مالك رضي الله عنه فيه رسالة قبل أن يولد الشافعي - كذا ، ولعله الأشعري - رضي الله عنه، وإنما نُسب للأشعري لأنه بيَّنَ مناهج الأوَّلين، ولخص موارد البراهين، ولم يحدث فيه بعد السلف إلا مجرد الألقاب والاصطلاحات، وقد حدث مثل ذلك في كل فن من فنون العلم، والقول بأن السلف نُهوا عن النظر فيه باطل، وإنما الذي نهوا عنه علم الجهمية والقدرية وغيرهم من أهل البدع، وهم الذين ذمَّهم الشافعي وغيره من السلف.
And the accomplished scholar Ibn Hajar al-Haytami said in his modern legal opinions on page 208 concerning the science of kalam: 'And what is said—that it is an innovation because the early predecessors (salaf) did not look into it, despite that it engenders contention, disputation, and doubts—is refuted by the fact that the salaf definitely looked into it. Among them were 'Umar and his son, 'Ali, and Ibn 'Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them, and among the followers (tabi'un) were 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz, Rabi'a, Ibn Hurmuz, Malik, and al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with them. And Malik, may Allah be pleased with him, wrote a treatise on it before al-Shafi'i was born—this is as stated, and perhaps it was al-Ash'ari—may Allah be pleased with him. And it was only attributed to al-Ash'ari because he elucidated the methodologies of the earlier scholars and summarized the sources of proofs. Nothing was introduced into it after the salaf except mere titles and terminologies, and such has occurred in every field of knowledge. And the claim that the salaf were prohibited from investigating it is false; rather, what they were prohibited from was the science of the Jahmiyya, the Qadariyya, and other people of innovation, and these are the ones whom al-Shafi'i and other salaf criticized.'
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage records Ibn Hajar al-Haytami's defense of the science of kalam against the accusation of being a blameworthy innovation (bid'a). The accusation is that the early righteous predecessors (salaf) did not engage in kalam and that it leads to disputes and doubts. Ibn Hajar refutes this by stating that the salaf did indeed practice kalam, and he lists prominent Companions (like 'Umar, his son 'Abdullah, 'Ali, Ibn 'Abbas) and successors (like 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz, Rabi'a, Ibn Hurmuz, Malik, al-Shafi'i) who engaged in it. He also notes that Imam Malik wrote a treatise on kalam before al-Shafi'i was born, though he adds a remark that perhaps it was al-Ash'ari (meaning the treatise is attributed to al-Ash'ari?). He clarifies that kalam is attributed to al-Ash'ari because he systematized the methods of earlier scholars and summarized proofs, not because he invented it. The only changes after the salaf were in terminology and classification, similar to other sciences. Finally, he asserts that the prohibition mentioned in the sources was directed specifically at the theological innovations of the Jahmiyya and Qadariyya, not at the defense of orthodox creed.

Correction: Ibn Hajar shows that many Companions and successors engaged in kalam, and the prohibition was specifically against the heretical sects like Jahmiyya and Qadariyya.

Key Takeaway
Kalam is not an innovation; it was practiced by the salaf and the prohibition was only against heretical theology.

The Origin of the Term Tasawwuf According to al-Qushayri
Qushayri narrates how the term tasawwuf emerged among the elite of Ahl al-Sunna.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term tasawwuf emerged historically among the elite of Ahl al-Sunna as a mark of spiritual vigilance, not as an innovation.

Core Idea
Tasawwuf was the title of the elite Sunnis guarding their hearts from heedlessness.

Imam al-Qushayri, a leading Ash'ari theologian and a link in the chains of both kalam and tasawwuf, traces the historical development of religious titles from the Companions to the Sufis. He shows that tasawwuf was not a later innovation but a natural designation for the most spiritually devoted among Ahl al-Sunna, especially after sects emerged and each claimed asceticism. Al-Shatibi's endorsement confirms this understanding against those who dismiss tasawwuf as foreign to Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the source and note the chain
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Qushayri begins by identifying himself within a scholarly lineage linking him to Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. This establishes his Sunni credentials.
The chain: Qushayri → Ibn Furak & al-Daqqaq → al-Bahili → al-Ash'ari.
2
Follow the historical titles
Trace the sequence: Companions (sahaba), Followers (tabi'un), Followers of the Followers (atba' al-tabi'in), then ascetics and worshippers (zuhhad wa 'ubbad), and finally tasawwuf.
Each title reflects the closeness to the Prophet's era.
3
Identify the trigger for 'tasawwuf'
Notice that the name tasawwuf emerged after innovations appeared and sects contested, with each claiming asceticism. The elite of Ahl al-Sunna then became distinct with that name.
It was a mark of authenticity, not a new sect.
4
Reflect on al-Shatibi's comment
Read al-Shatibi's statement that this title is specific to followers of the Sunna and distinguishes from innovation. This rebuts the misconception that tasawwuf is a bid'a.
5
Review the lesson's key takeaway
Summarize: Tasawwuf originally designated the spiritually vigilant Sunni elite, not a later innovation.
This lesson guards against dismissing tasawwuf as un-Islamic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لطيفة : قال الإمام القشيري - وهو من أعلام المتكلمين، ومن تلامذة الأستاذ ابن فورك والإمام الرباني أبي علي الدقاق، وهما من تلامذة الإمام علم السنة أبي الحسن الباهلي، وهو أبرز تلامذة شيخ السنة أبي الحسن الأشعري - في الرسالة القشيرية» (٣٤/١) : (اعلموا - رحمكم الله تعالى - أن المسلمين بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لم يتسم أفاضلهم في عصرهم بتسمية علم سوى صحبة رسول الله إذ لا فضيلة فوقها، فقيل لهم الصحابة، ولما أدركهم أهل العصر الثاني .. سمّى مَن صحب الصحابة : التابعين، ورأوا ذلك أشرف سمةٍ، ثم قيل لمن بعدهم : أتباع التابعين، ثم اختلف الناسُ، وتباينت المراتب ، فقيل لخواص الناس ممَّن لهم شدَّة عناية بأمر الدين: الزماد والعباد، ثم ظهرت البدعُ، وحصل التداعي بين الفرق فكلُّ فريق ادعوا أن فيهم زهداً، فانفرد خواص أهل السنة المراعون أنفاسَهُم مع الله تعالى الحافظون قلوبهم عن طوارق الغفلة باسم التصوُّف، واشتهر هذا الاسم لهؤلاء الأكابر قبل المئتين من الهجرة)، وقد نقل الإمام الشاطبي هذا النص في الاعتصام (۱۲۰/۱) وقال عقبه : (فقد عُدَّ هذا اللقب مخصوصاً باتباع السنة ومباينة البدعة، وفي ذلك ما يدلُّ على خلاف ما يعتقده الجهَّال ومن لا عبرة به من المدعين للعلم).
A subtle point: Imam al-Qushayri—who is among the notable theologians (al-mutakallimin), and among the students of the master Ibn Furak and the lordly Imam Abu 'Ali al-Daqqaq, both of whom were students of the Imam of the Sunna Abu al-Hasan al-Bahili, who is the most prominent student of the Shaykh of the Sunna Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari—said in al-Risala al-Qushayriyya (1/34): 'Know—may Allah have mercy on you—that after the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), the most excellent of the Muslims in their time did not designate themselves with any designation of knowledge other than the companionship of the Messenger of Allah, for there is no virtue above it. So they were called the Companions (al-sahaba). When the people of the second generation came, they called those who accompanied the Companions the Followers (al-tabi'un), and they considered that the noblest mark. Then those after them were called the Followers of the Followers (atba' al-tabi'in). Then people differed and ranks diverged. So the special ones among the people, those who had intense care for the matter of religion, were called the ascetics and worshippers (al-zuhhad wa al-'ubbad). Then innovations appeared, and contestation occurred between the sects, so every faction claimed that among them was asceticism. Then the elite of Ahl al-Sunna who observed their breaths with Allah and guarded their hearts from the intrusions of heedlessness became distinct with the name tasawwuf. And this name became famous for these great ones before the year 200 of the Hijra.' And Imam al-Shatibi transmitted this text in al-I'tisam (1/120) and said after it: 'Thus this title is considered specific to the followers of the Sunna and to distinguishing from innovation, and in that is evidence contrary to what the ignorant and those who are of no account among the claimants to knowledge believe.'
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a historical note (latifa) from Imam al-Qushayri, tracing the emergence of the term 'tasawwuf'. After praising the early generations who were simply called Companions, Followers, and Followers of the Followers, he explains that when people's ranks diverged, the most religiously devoted were called 'ascetics and worshippers'. Then, as sects arose and each claimed asceticism, the elite of Ahl al-Sunna—who were particularly mindful of their spiritual state—came to be known exclusively as 'tasawwuf'. This name became established before the second Islamic century. Al-Shatibi then confirms that this title is specifically for those who follow the Sunna and avoid innovation, contrary to the claims of ignorant pretenders.

Key Terms
التصوُّف
أهل السنة
الزهاد والعباد
البدعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tasawwuf is an innovation (bid'a) that has no basis in early Islam and is separate from Ahl al-Sunna.
Correction: Al-Qushayri shows that tasawwuf emerged among the elite of Ahl al-Sunna before the year 200 AH as a mark of distinguishing true asceticism from the claims of sects, and al-Shatibi affirms that it is specific to followers of the Sunna.

Checkpoint
According to al-Qushayri, what caused the elite of Ahl al-Sunna to be distinguished by the name tasawwuf?
A) The end of the era of the Companions
B) The appearance of innovations and contestation between sects
C) The death of the Prophet
D) A command from the caliphs

Key Takeaway
The term tasawwuf was historically a badge of honor for the most spiritually vigilant followers of the Sunna, not a later innovation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you among those who guard their hearts from heedlessness, or do you let distractions overtake you? What specific step will you take today to protect your heart from ghaflah?
?
The title of tasawwuf was given to those who intensely cared for the matter of religion. Does your daily life reflect an intense care for your deen, or is it a side concern? How can you make it your primary concern starting from today's prayer?
?
Al-Qushayri mentions that every faction claimed asceticism. How can you ensure your spiritual efforts are grounded in the Sunna and not just personal desire? Are you following the path of the elite of Ahl al-Sunna, or are you content with a superficial religiosity that lacks true vigilance?

The Linguistic and Technical Meanings of al-Din
Lists linguistic meanings then gives concise technical definition of din.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic meanings of the word 'din' and its concise technical definition, as well as the related terms millah, shar', and shari'ah, and the distinction between real and metaphorical legislation.

Core Idea
Al-Din is both a universal concept and a revealed system—Allah alone is the real Legislator.

Before diving into the doctrinal details of the Jawharah, we need to clarify exactly what is meant by the term 'al-din.' This word carries multiple linguistic shades—obedience, worship, recompense—and a precise technical meaning as the rulings Allah revealed through His prophet. The author also explains why we call it millah, shar', and shari'ah, and cautions that only Allah is the true legislator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the list of linguistic meanings and the definition.
The passage is concise; the author builds from language to technical usage.
2
Identify the linguistic and technical dimensions
Find the four linguistic meanings listed. Then locate the concise technical definition. Notice the terms din, millah, shar', shari'ah and how they relate.
The same reality is called by different names depending on perspective.
3
Reflect on the real vs. metaphorical legislator
The passage ends by emphasizing that Allah is the real legislator and the prophet is metaphorical. Why is this distinction important? Think about what it guards against.
This prevents attributing divine authority to the prophet in the same way as Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (الدِّينُ) يطلق لغةً على عدة معانٍ؛ منها الطاعة والعبادة والجزاء والحساب (٥)، ولهم فيه اصطلاحاً تعريفان: أحدُهُما مختصر: وهو ما شرعَهُ اللهُ تعالى على لسان نبيه من الأحكام، ويسمى ديناً لأننا ندين له وننقاد، ويسمّى أيضاً ملَّةً من حيث إن الملك يُمليه على الرسول، وهو يُمليه علينا ويسمى شرعاً وشريعة من حيث إن الله شرعَه لنا؛ أي: بيَّنه لنا على لسان النبي؛ فالله هو الشارع حقيقة، والنبي شارع مجاز (٦)
His saying: (al-din) is used linguistically for several meanings: among them obedience, worship, recompense, and reckoning (5). And in technical usage they have two definitions: one of them is concise: it is that which Allah Almighty has legislated upon the tongue of His prophet from the rulings, and it is called din because we are submissive and obedient to it, and it is also called millah from the perspective that the King dictates it to the messenger, and he dictates it to us, and it is called shar' and shari'ah from the perspective that Allah has legislated it for us, i.e., made it clear to us upon the tongue of the prophet; so Allah is the real legislator, and the prophet is a metaphorical legislator (6).
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a definition passage. It first gives the linguistic meanings of the word 'din': obedience, worship, recompense, and reckoning. Then it states that technically there are two definitions. The first is concise: it is the legislation (ahkam) that Allah sent through the Prophet. The author notes that 'din' is also called 'millah' because Allah dictates it to the messenger, who then dictates to us. It is also called 'shar'' or 'shari'ah' because Allah made it clear through the prophet. He emphasizes that Allah is the real legislator, while the prophet is a metaphorical legislator because the prophet is the means of conveying the legislation. This distinction guards against attributing legislation to the prophet in the same sense as Allah.

Key Terms
دين
مختصر
ملة
شرع
شريعة
حقيقة
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) is a legislator in the same sense as Allah, i.e., that he can independently create religious rulings.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the prophet is only a metaphorical legislator; he conveys and explains what Allah legislated. Allah alone is the real legislator (al-shari' haqiqah).

Checkpoint
How many technical definitions of al-din are mentioned in this passage?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Al-din linguistically means obedience, worship, recompense, and reckoning, but technically it refers to the rulings Allah legislated through the prophet—and only Allah is the true legislator.

?
If al-din means obedience and submission to Allah alone, which area of your life today do you find hardest to submit to His law? What one concrete change can you make this week to bring that area into obedience?
?
Since only Allah is the real legislator, how often do you catch yourself following cultural norms or personal desires over Allah's shari'ah? What is one specific norm you will challenge starting today?
?
The prophet is a metaphorical legislator—do you sometimes treat his sunnah as if it were merely his own opinion rather than divine revelation? How can you increase your reverence for the sunnah as the very words of Allah conveyed through His messenger?

The Extended Definition of Din: Divine Positing and Its Components
Extended definition of din with explanation of wad', ilahi, sa'iq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed definition of al-din as a divine positing compelling sound intellects toward intrinsic good, and how each component (wad', ilahi, sa'iq) shapes the meaning.

Core Idea
Din is a divine positing that compels us toward what is truly good.

The author now offers a more precise definition of din, breaking it down into three key terms: wad' (positing), ilahi (divine), and sa'iq (compelling). This definition carefully distinguishes religious law from human-made systems and clarifies the role of human effort in discovering divine rulings.

Guided Path
1
Read the extended definition
Start by reading the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three key terms: وضع, إلهي, سائق.
The definition includes a parenthetical objection about ijtihad – pay attention to how it fits.
2
Understand the meaning of 'wad'
The author explains that 'wad' is a verbal noun meaning something posited. It is broad to include rulings and other things, and metaphor is used due to prevalence.
This allows the definition to cover all divine institutions.
3
Grasp why 'ilahi' excludes human positing
The term 'ilahi' excludes purely human systems like political laws or crafts. An objection about ijtihad is answered: mujtahids discover, not create, divine rulings.
Human acquisition (kasb) does not make a ruling non-divine.
4
See how 'sa'iq' adds motivation
The term 'sa'iq' means impelling, based on rewards and punishments. It excludes non-compelling divine acts like natural phenomena.
This highlights the moral and motivational aspect of din.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما مطوَّلٌ: وهو وضع إلهي سائق لذوي العقول السليمة باختيارهم المحمود إلى ما هو خير لهم بالذات (١). فقولهم: (وضع) أي موضوع، فهو مصدر بمعنى اسم المفعول؛ أي شيءٌ موضوع بقطع النظر عن أن يكونَ حُكْماً أو غيره لأجل الإخراجات الآتية، ودخل المجاز التعريف لشهرته (٢). وقولهم: (إلهي) أي: منسوب للإله؛ وهو الله تعالى، وخرج به الوضع البشري ظاهراً، وإلا فالواضع لجميع الأشياء هو الله في الحقيقة، وذلك نحو الرسوم السياسية؛ أي: القوانين التي ترجع إليها سياسة العالم؛ كعلم إصلاح المنزل، وحسن العشرة مع الأهل والإخوان، والأوضاع الصناعية كالتجارة والقزازة وغير ذلك، وقد كانت الحكماء القدماء يؤلفون كتباً في سياسة الرعية وإصلاح المدن، فيحكم بها ملوك من لا شرعَ لهم، فإنه وإن كان الخالقُ لكل الأشياء هو الله تعالى، إلا أن البشر لهم في هذه كسب. لا يقال: يلزم على ذلك أن أحكام الفقه الاجتهادية ليست من الدين؛ لأنَّ البشر أعني المجتهدين - لهم فيها كسب، وإنما منه ما ورد نصّا لا خلاف فيه؛ لأنا نقول: هي من الدين قطعاً، وهي موضوع إلهي غاية الأمر أنه يخفى علينا، والمجتهدون يعانون إظهارها والاستدلال عليها بقواعد الشرع، ولا مدخل لهم في وضعها (٣). وقولهم: (سائق) أي: باعث وحامل؛ لأنَّ المكلَّف إذا سمع ما يترتب على فعل الواجب من الثواب أو على فعل الحرام من العقاب انساق إلى فعل الأول وتركِ الثاني وهكذا قالوا (٤) وخرج به الوضع الإلهي غيرُ السائق؛ كإنبات الأرض وإمطار هر[؟]
And the second definition is an extended one: it is a divine positing compelling those of sound intellects, by their praiseworthy choice, toward what is intrinsically good for them (1). So their saying: (wad') means a positing; it is a verbal noun in the sense of the passive participle; i.e., something posited regardless of whether it is a ruling or something else for the sake of the exclusions to follow, and metaphorical usage entered the definition because of its prevalence (2). And their saying: (ilahi) i.e., relative to the deity; and He is Allah Almighty, and by it human positing is excluded apparently, for otherwise the positer of all things is Allah in reality, and that includes things like political statutes, i.e., the laws to which the governance of the world refers, such as the science of household management, good companionship with family and brothers, and industrial crafts like commerce and glassblowing and so forth, and the ancient philosophers used to compose books on the policy of subjects and the reform of cities, which were enforced by kings who had no divine law, for although the Creator of all things is Allah Almighty, yet humans have acquisition (kasb) in these things. Do not say: from that it follows that the rulings of ijtihad in fiqh are not part of the religion, because humans, i.e., the mujtahids, have acquisition in them, and only what comes as a clear text without dispute is part of it; for we say: they are definitely part of the religion, and they are a divine positing, only it is hidden from us, and the mujtahids are tasked with manifesting them and inferring them by the rules of the Shari'ah, and they have no role in their institution (3). And their saying: (sa'iq) i.e., impeller and motivator; because the responsible person, when he hears what results from performing the obligatory act of reward or from committing the forbidden act of punishment, is impelled to perform the first and avoid the second, and so they said (4), and by it is excluded the non-compelling divine positing, like the growth of the earth and the raining of [?].
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation passage. It presents the second, more detailed, definition of al-din: a divine positing (wad' ilahi) that compels (sa'iq) people of sound intellects, through their praiseworthy choice, to what is intrinsically good for them. The explanation unpacks each key term. First, 'wad' means something that is placed (maudu') and here it is a verbal noun used in the sense of the passive participle. This broad term allows the definition to cover not just rulings but anything posited, and metaphor is used because of its prevalence. Second, 'ilahi' means divine, excluding purely human positing, though ultimately all positing comes from Allah, but humans have kasb (acquisition) in man-made laws like political statutes or crafts. This leads to an objection: if human effort (kasb) is involved, then ijtihad rulings might not be divine. The answer is that ijtihad rulings are still divine because the mujtahid only discovers what is already divinely instituted, not creates it. Third, 'sa'iq' means impelling or motivating, based on the rewards and punishments of the rulings, which drives the responsible person (al-mukallaf) to act. This excludes non-compelling divine acts like natural phenomena (growth of earth, rain). Thus the definition precisely captures the nature of religious law.

Key Terms
وضع
إلهي
سائق
اختيار محمود
خير بالذات
مجاز
مجتهد
كسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That ijtihad rulings are not part of din because they involve human effort (kasb).
Correction: The passage explains that ijtihad rulings are definitely part of din; they are divine positing, hidden from us, and mujtahids merely manifest and infer them through Shari'ah rules, with no role in their institution.

Checkpoint
According to the detailed definition, what is excluded by the term 'ilahi' (divine)?
A) The rulings of ijtihad
B) Human positing like political laws
C) The growth of plants
D) The rain

Key Takeaway
The detailed definition of din as wad' ilahi sa'iq precisely captures divine law, distinguishing it from human systems and emphasizing its motivational power through reward and punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that even the rulings derived by scholars through ijtihad are divinely posited, not human inventions. Does this change how you listen to a scholar's fatwa? Will you now receive it with greater reverence, knowing it is ultimately from Allah, even if discovered through human effort?
?
The definition says din compels through the promise of reward and threat of punishment. Ask yourself: when you pray or avoid sin, are you truly driven by that divine sa'iq? Or have you become desensitized to the incentives? What would it take for you to feel the weight of reward and punishment in your daily actions?
?
What custom or law do you follow that you have never questioned whether it is from Allah or merely human? Are you willing to examine it in light of this definition? Commit to investigating one area of your life where you may have replaced divine guidance with human convention—and take a step to realign it today.

Linguistic vs. Technical Meaning of Tawḥīd: Resolving Poetic Repetition
The commentator resolves an apparent rhyme repetition by distinguishing two meanings of tawḥīd and discussing poetic rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the poet against the charge of īṭā' by distinguishing the linguistic and technical meanings of tawḥīd and by applying the rules of Arabic prosody.

Core Idea
Poetic repetition of a word is not always a flaw—it can be a deliberate device.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a potential flaw in the poem: the word 'tawḥīd' appears twice close together, which might be considered a poetic fault called īṭā'. He explores several ways to resolve this, from distinguishing meanings to invoking the rules of verse meter. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Notice that the word 'tawḥīd' appears twice in close proximity. The commentator worries this might be considered a poetic flaw called īṭā' (identical rhyme). Understand what īṭā' means.
Īṭā' is the repetition of the same rhyme word with the same meaning within fewer than seven verses.
2
First resolution: linguistic vs. technical meanings
The commentator suggests that the first occurrence is technical (referring to the theological concept) and the second is linguistic (simply meaning 'oneness'). If so, the meanings differ, so no īṭā'.
3
Second resolution: consider the meter
When this first resolution is refuted, he invokes the meter. He explains that īṭā' only applies if the poem is in the 'half-verse' (mashṭūr) rajaz meter; if it is full (tāmm), the end of the first half is not a rhyme, so repetition there does not count.
4
Final alternatives
Even if īṭā' were conceded, it is permissible for modern poets. Or, if the meanings differ, it is a different figure of speech: jinās (paronomasia).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (عَنِ التَّوْحِيدِ) متعلّق بـ (خلا) والمراد بالتوحيد هنا : التوحيد اللغوي؛ وهو العلم بأن الشيء واحد وبحمل التوحيد هنا على اللغوي وفيما مرَّ على الشرعي، اندفع الإيطاءُ ؛ وهو اتحاد القافيتين لفظاً ومعنى فيما دون سبعة أبيات، ورُدَّ ذلك بأن الدين إنما عرا عن التوحيد الشرعي ؛ فالحقُّ أن التوحيد في الموضعين شرعي، ولا يرد أن في كلامه إيطاء إلا إذا كانت هذه المقدمة من مشطور الرجز، أما إذا كانت من تامه، فلا إيطاء؛ لما علمت من أنه اتحاد القافيتين، وقافيه البيت لا تكون إلا آخره، أما آخرُ الشطر الأول، فليس بقافية، قال شيخ الإسلام: (خرج بتكرير القافية تكرير غيرها؛ كتكرير آخرِ النصف الأول مع آخر بيت؛ فليس بإيطاء)(١) ، ولو سُلّم أن في كلام المصنف إيطاء، فهو جائز للمولدين كما هو جائز لغيرهم، وعلى اختلاف التوحيد في الموضعين يكون في الكلام الجناس التام ؛ وهو اتفاق الكلمتين لفظاً لا معنى.
His statement: 'about al-tawḥīd' is connected to 'khalā'. And the intended meaning of tawḥīd here is the linguistic tawḥīd, which is the knowledge that something is one. And by taking tawḥīd here as linguistic and earlier as technical, the īṭā' is repelled; and īṭā' is the agreement of two rhymes in wording and meaning within fewer than seven verses. And that was refuted by [the argument] that religion is indeed empty of technical tawḥīd; so the truth is that tawḥīd in both places is technical. And it is not a problem that there is īṭā' in his speech unless this introduction is from the half-verse type of rajaz; but if it is from the full type, then there is no īṭā', because you know that īṭā' is the agreement of two rhymes, and the rhyme of the verse is only its end, while the end of the first half is not a rhyme. Shaykh al-Islam said: 'Repetition of the rhyme excludes the repetition of other things; like the repetition of the end of the first half with the end of a verse; it is not īṭā'.' And even if it is conceded that there is īṭā' in the author's speech, it is permissible for mawlids as it is permissible for others. And if the meaning of tawḥīd differs in the two places, then there is complete jinās in the speech, which is the agreement of two words in wording but not meaning.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage deals with a potential flaw in the poem: the word 'tawḥīd' appears twice close together, raising suspicion of īṭā' (identical rhyme). The commentator first suggests that tawḥīd is used linguistically in the later occurrence and technically earlier, thus avoiding īṭā'. But this is refuted because religion cannot be devoid of technical tawḥīd. So both are technical. Then he argues that īṭā' only applies if the poem's meter is the half-verse rajaz; if full, the first half's end is not a rhyme. He quotes Shaykh al-Islam to support that repetition of a word in a non-rhyme position is not īṭā'. Finally, even if it were īṭā', it is permissible for modern poets. Alternatively, if the meanings differ, it becomes jinās (paronomasia).

Key Terms
توحيد
إيطاء
جناس
قافية
مشطور الرجز
تام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any repetition of the same word in a poem is a poetic flaw (īṭā').
Correction: Īṭā' is specifically the repetition of the rhyme word with the same meaning within fewer than seven verses. Repetition of non-rhyme words, or with different meanings (jinās), is not īṭā'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does the repetition of a word become īṭā'?
A) Whenever the same word appears twice in a poem
B) When the same word appears as the rhyme of two verses with the same meaning within less than seven verses
C) Only in rajaz meter
D) Only when the poet intends it

Key Takeaway
The commentator shows that an apparent poetic defect can be resolved through careful analysis of word meanings and poetic rules, and that even if a defect exists, it may be permissible or transform into another figure of speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a seeming contradiction in a religious text, do you rush to a superficial explanation, or do you, like the commentator, carefully examine the linguistic and contextual nuances? Which approach better honors the depth of knowledge? Will you commit today to pausing before dismissing an apparent inconsistency?
?
The commentator defends the author's wording instead of assuming a flaw. In your own engagement with scholars or texts, do you assume good faith and seek deeper understanding, or do you quickly judge? How might this lesson transform your attitude toward the works of scholars you find hard to understand?
?
This passage shows that a word's meaning can shift based on context. Have you ever misunderstood someone's speech because you assumed a word meant the same as in a previous usage? Will you from now on actively consider the possibility that identical terms may carry different intentions, especially in matters of creed?

The Ruling on Salawat upon the Prophet's Family
Commentary on salawat ruling and grammatical coordination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ruling on sending salawat upon the family of the Prophet, both as a part of salawat on him and independently.

Core Idea
Sending salawat on the family is demanded when following the Prophet but disliked independently.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a common point of confusion: how to properly phrase salawat upon the Prophet and his family. He first addresses the grammatical coordination, then explains the permissibility and recommendation of sending salawat on the family as part of the salawat on the Prophet, and finally discusses the ruling when done independently.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the translation to understand the literal meaning.
2
Understand the grammatical coordination
Notice that the commentator argues that 'wa alihi' should be coordinated to 'nabi' (prophet) rather than to 'Muhammad'. Understand the reasoning: since 'Muhammad' is a substitute for 'nabi', coordinating the family to 'Muhammad' would make them a substitute too, which is incorrect.
This grammatical point affects the meaning of the salawat phrase.
3
Distinguish between secondary and independent salawat
Identify two scenarios: sending salawat on the family as part of salawat on the Prophet (permissible and demanded) vs. independently (disliked according to the most correct view).
The 'bater prayer' is one that omits the family.
4
Note the ruling on salam (greeting)
Learn that al-Juwayni annexed 'salam' to 'salah' for absent persons, but addressed persons receive direct greeting phrases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَآلِهِ ... إلى آخره)؛ أي وسلام الله مع صلاته على آله ... إلى آخره، فهو معطوف على (نبي) كما هو المتعين، وأما عطفه على (محمد) فلا يخفى فساده، وإن ذكره المصنف في «شرحه» ؛ لأن محمداً) بدل من ، نبي)، والمعطوف على البدل بدل ، ولا يصح أن يكون الآلُ ومَنْ ذُكر معهم بدلاً من (نبي) (۱) وفي كلامه الصلاة على غير الأنبياء والملائكة تبعاً، وهي جائزة اتفاقاً، بل هي مطلوبةٌ ؛ لقوله : «اللهم صل على محمد وعلى آلِ محمد (٢)، وللنهي عن الصلاة البتراء ؛ وهي التي لم يُذكر فيها الآلُ، وأما استقلالاً فقيل بأنها ممنوعة، وقيل: مكروهة، وقيل: خلاف الأولى، والأصح الكراهة وألحق أبو محمد الجويني السلام بالصلاة بالنظر للغائب، وأما المخاطب فيخاطب بالسلام عليك أو عليكم أو نحوه (۳). وأصل (آل) : أَوّل كَجَمَل ؛ بدليل تصغيره على (أويل)، وقيل: أصله أهل؛ بدليل (۱) فلو قلنا : جاءَ الإمام علي والخادم، ثم جعلنا (الخادم) معطوفاً على (علي) للزم أن يكون الخادم إماماً ؛ لأن المعطوف على البدل بدل أيضاً ، أما عطفه على الإمام) فلا يضر، بل هو المتعين، وهذا ليس كقولنا : جاء الإمام أبو يوسف ومحمد، ومع هذا فقد قال العلامة عبد البر الأجهوري في فتح المجيد : (وآله): معطوف على نبي أو محمد، لكن عطفهم على نبي أولى؛ لأنه هو المحدّث عنه بطريق الأصالة، ومحمد بدل منه . (٢) أخرجه البخاري (٦٣٥٧) ، ومسلم (٤٠٦). (۳) في (ب): (ونحوه).
His saying: (and his family ... etc.) i.e., and the peace of Allah with His blessings upon his family ... etc., so it is coordinated to (nabi) as is required, but as for coordinating it to (Muhammad), its corruption is not hidden, even if the author mentioned it in his commentary, because (Muhammad) is a substitute for (nabi), and what is coordinated to the substitute is a substitute, and it is not correct that the family and those mentioned with them are a substitute for (nabi). (1) And in his speech, sending salawat on other than prophets and angels is secondary, and it is permissible by consensus, rather it is demanded because of his saying: 'O Allah, send prayers upon Muhammad and upon the family of Muhammad' (2), and because of the prohibition of the 'bater prayer' (al-salat al-batra'), which is that in which the family is not mentioned. As for sending salawat independently (without conjunction), some say it is prohibited, some say it is disliked (makruh), some say it is against the best (khilaf al-awla), and the most correct is that it is disliked. Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni annexed greeting (salam) to prayer (salah) with respect to the absent person, but the one addressed is addressed with 'alayka' or 'alaykum' or similar. (3) The origin of (al) is awwal like jamal, evidenced by its diminutive 'uwail', and it is said its origin is 'ahl' evidenced by (1) For if we say: 'The Imam Ali came and the servant', then we make (the servant) coordinated to (Ali), it would necessitate that the servant be an imam, because what is coordinated to the substitute is also a substitute. But coordinating it to (the Imam) does not harm, rather it is the required. And this is not like our saying: 'The Imam Abu Yusuf came and Muhammad'. And despite this, al-Allama Abd al-Barr al-Ujhuri said in Fath al-Majid: (and his family) is coordinated to nabi or Muhammad, but coordinating them to nabi is better because he is the one being spoken about primarily, and Muhammad is a substitute from him. (2) Recorded by al-Bukhari (6357) and Muslim (406). (3) In copy (b): (and similar).
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and jurisprudential analysis. The commentator first examines the syntax of 'wa alihi': it is correctly coordinated to 'nabi' (prophet), not to 'Muhammad' which is a substitute. Then he discusses the ruling on sending salawat upon the family: when following salawat upon the Prophet, it is permissible and even demanded; but independently, the most correct view is that it is disliked (makruh). He also notes that al-Juwayni annexed salam to salah for absent persons. Finally, he gives the etymology of 'Al': from 'awwal' (like jamal) based on its diminutive 'uwail', or from 'ahl'.

Key Terms
الصلاة
السلام
الآل
الصلاة البتراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That sending salawat on the family of the Prophet is always recommended, even independently.
Correction: The correct view is that while it is demanded as part of salawat on the Prophet, doing so independently is disliked (makruh).

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on sending salawat upon the family of the Prophet independently (without mentioning the Prophet)?
A) Permissible and recommended.
B) Prohibited.
C) Disliked (makruh) according to the most correct view.
D) Obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Sending salawat on the family is demanded when following the Prophet but disliked if done independently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite salawat in your prayers or on your own, do you consciously include the family? If not, what does this reveal about the depth of your love for the Prophet's household? Will you commit to saying the full formula from now on?
?
The commentary warns that sending salawat on the family independently may be disliked. Have you ever performed such an act without knowledge? How can you protect your worship from falling into disliked or prohibited actions out of ignorance?
?
The 'bater prayer' is incomplete without the family. Reflect on your own salah: are there any incomplete acts you perform out of habit? What will you change to ensure your worship is perfect in the eyes of Allah?

The Etymology of 'Al' and Related Grammatical Notes
Linguistic etymology and grammatical illustration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic origin of the word 'Al' (family) and the grammatical principle regarding coordination to substitutes.

Core Idea
The word 'Al' originates from 'awwal' or 'ahl', and grammatical rules affect its usage.

This passage delves into the etymology of the Arabic word 'Al', meaning family or household. The commentator presents two possible origins and supports them with linguistic evidence. He also revisits the grammatical point about coordination to substitutes, using an illustrative example to clarify the earlier ruling.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage carefully, noting the two proposed etymologies for 'Al'.
2
Understand the first etymology
The first origin is 'awwal' (first/leader), with evidence from the diminutive 'uwail'. This is presented as the primary view.
The analogy is 'jamal' (camel) whose diminutive is 'jumail'.
3
Understand the second etymology
The second origin is 'ahl' (people/family), which is the more common understanding.
4
Learn the grammatical rule about coordination
The commentator gives an example: if you say 'The Imam Ali came and the servant', coordinating 'the servant' to 'Ali' would make the servant an imam, because 'Ali' is a substitute for 'the imam'. Thus, coordination to the substitute is problematic.
This reinforces the earlier point about coordinating 'wa alihi' to 'Muhammad'.

قوله : (وَآلِهِ ... إلى آخره)؛ أي وسلام الله مع صلاته على آله ... إلى آخره، فهو معطوف على (نبي) كما هو المتعين، وأما عطفه على (محمد) فلا يخفى فساده، وإن ذكره المصنف في «شرحه» ؛ لأن محمداً) بدل من ، نبي)، والمعطوف على البدل بدل ، ولا يصح أن يكون الآلُ ومَنْ ذُكر معهم بدلاً من (نبي) (۱) وفي كلامه الصلاة على غير الأنبياء والملائكة تبعاً، وهي جائزة اتفاقاً، بل هي مطلوبةٌ ؛ لقوله : «اللهم صل على محمد وعلى آلِ محمد (٢)، وللنهي عن الصلاة البتراء ؛ وهي التي لم يُذكر فيها الآلُ، وأما استقلالاً فقيل بأنها ممنوعة، وقيل: مكروهة، وقيل: خلاف الأولى، والأصح الكراهة وألحق أبو محمد الجويني السلام بالصلاة بالنظر للغائب، وأما المخاطب فيخاطب بالسلام عليك أو عليكم أو نحوه (۳). وأصل (آل) : أَوّل كَجَمَل ؛ بدليل تصغيره على (أويل)، وقيل: أصله أهل؛ بدليل (۱) فلو قلنا : جاءَ الإمام علي والخادم، ثم جعلنا (الخادم) معطوفاً على (علي) للزم أن يكون الخادم إماماً ؛ لأن المعطوف على البدل بدل أيضاً ، أما عطفه على الإمام) فلا يضر، بل هو المتعين، وهذا ليس كقولنا : جاء الإمام أبو يوسف ومحمد، ومع هذا فقد قال العلامة عبد البر الأجهوري في فتح المجيد : (وآله): معطوف على نبي أو محمد، لكن عطفهم على نبي أولى؛ لأنه هو المحدّث عنه بطريق الأصالة، ومحمد بدل منه . (٢) أخرجه البخاري (٦٣٥٧) ، ومسلم (٤٠٦). (۳) في (ب): (ونحوه).
His saying: (and his family ... etc.) i.e., and the peace of Allah with His blessings upon his family ... etc., so it is coordinated to (nabi) as is required, but as for coordinating it to (Muhammad), its corruption is not hidden, even if the author mentioned it in his commentary, because (Muhammad) is a substitute for (nabi), and what is coordinated to the substitute is a substitute, and it is not correct that the family and those mentioned with them are a substitute for (nabi). (1) And in his speech, sending salawat on other than prophets and angels is secondary, and it is permissible by consensus, rather it is demanded because of his saying: 'O Allah, send prayers upon Muhammad and upon the family of Muhammad' (2), and because of the prohibition of the 'bater prayer' (al-salat al-batra'), which is that in which the family is not mentioned. As for sending salawat independently (without conjunction), some say it is prohibited, some say it is disliked (makruh), some say it is against the best (khilaf al-awla), and the most correct is that it is disliked. Abu Muhammad al-Juwayni annexed greeting (salam) to prayer (salah) with respect to the absent person, but the one addressed is addressed with 'alayka' or 'alaykum' or similar. (3) The origin of (al) is awwal like jamal, evidenced by its diminutive 'uwail', and it is said its origin is 'ahl' evidenced by (1) For if we say: 'The Imam Ali came and the servant', then we make (the servant) coordinated to (Ali), it would necessitate that the servant be an imam, because what is coordinated to the substitute is also a substitute. But coordinating it to (the Imam) does not harm, rather it is the required. And this is not like our saying: 'The Imam Abu Yusuf came and Muhammad'. And despite this, al-Allama Abd al-Barr al-Ujhuri said in Fath al-Majid: (and his family) is coordinated to nabi or Muhammad, but coordinating them to nabi is better because he is the one being spoken about primarily, and Muhammad is a substitute from him. (2) Recorded by al-Bukhari (6357) and Muslim (406). (3) In copy (b): (and similar).
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and jurisprudential analysis. The commentator first examines the syntax of 'wa alihi': it is correctly coordinated to 'nabi' (prophet), not to 'Muhammad' which is a substitute. Then he discusses the ruling on sending salawat upon the family: when following salawat upon the Prophet, it is permissible and even demanded; but independently, the most correct view is that it is disliked (makruh). He also notes that al-Juwayni annexed salam to salah for absent persons. Finally, he gives the etymology of 'Al': from 'awwal' (like jamal) based on its diminutive 'uwail', or from 'ahl'.

Linguistic Analysis of the Transition Phrase 'Wa-Ba'du'
Analyzes the word 'Wa-Ba'du' as a transition phrase, its construction, and origins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical construction, function, and origins of the transition phrase 'Wa-Ba'du' as used in classical Arabic texts.

Core Idea
'Wa-Ba'du' is a specialized transition phrase with deep grammatical roots.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the word 'Wa-Ba'du' that appears in the poem's opening. He explains its grammatical state, its function as a transition marker, and traces its origins back to the Sunnah and classical Arabic constructions. This will help you appreciate the precision of classical Arabic composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Notice the technical terms used.
2
Identify the three grammatical explanations
The passage presents two possible constructions for 'Wa-Ba'du' (indeclinable with dammah, or accusative without nunation) and then its function as a transition. Find each one and note the reasoning.
Also find the two origins mentioned: 'Amma Ba'd' and 'Mahma yakun min shay'in ba'd'.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this linguistic detail matters for understanding Islamic texts. The origins trace back to the Prophet's practice, showing the importance of precision.
This connects to the learning objective about the phrase's role in classical texts.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وَبَعْدُ) بالبناء على الضم لحذف المضاف إليه ونيَّة معناه)، والتقدير : وبعد البسملة(۲) والحمدلة والصلاة والسلام على النبي وآله وصحبه وحزبه، ويحتمل أن يكونَ بالنصب من غير تنوين لحذف المضاف إليه ونية لفظه، لكن المشهور على الألسنة الأول، وهي كلمة يُؤتى بها للانتقال من أسلوب إلى أسلوب آخر; أي: من نوع من الكلام إلى نوع آخر، والنوع المنتقلُ منه هو البسملة وما بعدها، والمنتقل إليه هو بيان السبب الحامل على التأليف. وأصلها الثاني : (أما بعد) ; بدليل لزوم الفاء في حيّزها غالباً، وهذا الأصل هو السنة; فقد كان يأتي بها في خطبه ومراسلاته (۳) ; وصح أنه خطب فقال: «أما بعد». والأصل الأصيل مهما يكن مِنْ شيءٍ بعد) (٤) ; فمهما: اسم شرط مبتدأ،
His saying: 'Wa-Ba'du' (and after) is indeclinable with dammah due to the omission of the genitive complement and the intention of its meaning. The estimation is: 'And after the Basmalah (2) and the Hamdalah and the prayer and peace upon the Prophet, his family, his Companions, and his party.' And it is possible that it is in the accusative without nunation due to the omission of the genitive complement and the intention of its wording, but the well-known on tongues is the first. And it is a word used to transition from one style to another, i.e., from one type of speech to another. The type transitioned from is the Basmalah and what follows it, and the type transitioned to is the explanation of the reason that motivated the authorship. And its second origin is 'Amma Ba'd' (as for after), evidenced by the necessity of 'fa' in its place mostly, and this origin is the Sunnah; for he (the Prophet) used to use it in his sermons and letters (3); and it is established that he gave a sermon and said: 'Amma Ba'd'. And the most original origin is 'Mahma yakun min shay'in ba'd' (4); so 'mahma' is a conditional noun in the nominative as the subject...
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed linguistic analysis of the word 'Wa-Ba'du' as used in the opening line of the poem. The author first explains its construction: it is indeclinable with a dammah because the genitive complement is omitted but its meaning is intended. The implicit meaning is 'after the Basmalah, Hamdalah, and prayer upon the Prophet, his family, Companions, and party'. An alternative construction is accusative without nunation if the wording is intended, but the first is more common. The function of this word is to transition from one stylistic section to another—specifically, from the opening invocations to stating the reason for composing the poem. The author then traces its origins: the second origin is 'Amma Ba'd', which is proven by the frequent attachment of the particle 'fa' to it, and this usage is attested in the Prophet's sermons and letters. The most original origin is 'Mahma yakun min shay'in ba'd', where 'mahma' functions as a conditional noun.

Grammatical Construction and Function of 'Wa Ba'du'
Explanation of the grammatical construction and function of 'wa ba'du'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical rule for the vowel ending of 'wa ba'du', its implied meaning, and its role as a transition marker from one section of speech to another.

Core Idea
'Wa ba'du' is a grammatically fixed phrase marking a shift from introduction to main content.

The commentator begins by analyzing the very first word of the poem, 'wa ba'du', explaining why it is built on damma and what it really means. This lesson unpacks that grammar and clarifies how this short phrase functions as a bridge between the opening invocations and the author's purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the construction of 'wa ba'du'
Read the Arabic text and its translation focusing on the two possible vowel endings (damma vs. accusative) and why the damma is preferred.
Notice the technical terms: 'بناء على الضم' and 'حذف المضاف إليه'.
2
Understand the transition function
Identify the two types of speech being connected: the basmala et al. and the statement of the reason for writing. See how 'wa ba'du' signals a change in topic.
3
Relate to your own knowledge
Think of similar transitional phrases in other languages (e.g., 'Now then,' 'Moreover') and consider why such set phrases exist.
This helps internalize the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَبَعْدُ فَالعِلْمُ بأَصْلِ الدِّينِ مُحَنَّمٌ يَحْتَاجُ لِلنَّ قوله: (وَبَعْدُ) بالبناء على الضم لحذف المضاف إليه ونيَّة معناه، والتقدير : وبعد البسملة والحمدلة والصلاة والسلام على النبي وآله وصحبه وحزبه، ويحتمل أن يكونَ بالنصب من غير تنوين لحذف المضاف إليه ونية لفظه، لكن المشهور على الألسنة الأول، وهي كلمة يُؤتى بها للانتقال من أسلوب إلى أسلوب آخر؛ أي: من نوع من الكلام إلى نوع آخر، والنوع المنتقلُ منه هو البسملة وما بعدها، والمنتقل إليه هو بيان السبب الحامل على التأليف. وأصلها الثاني : (أما بعد) ؛ بدليل لزوم الفاء في حيّزها غالباً، وهذا الأصل هو السنة؛ فقد كان يأتي بها في خطبه ومراسلاته؛ وصح أنه خطب فقال: «أما بعد». والأصل الأصيل مهما يكن مِنْ شيءٍ بعد؛ فمهما: اسم شرط مبتدأ، ويكن فعل الشرط، وهو مضارعُ (كانَ) التامَّةِ ، وفاعله ضمير مستتر تقديره هو يعود على (مهما) و (من شيء) بيان لمهما وإن كان شأن البيان التخصيص؛ فقد يكونُ مساوياً إشارة إلى أن المراد الجنس بتمامه، فحذفت (مهما) و(يكن) و(من شيء)، وأقيمت (أما ) مقام ذلك، ثم إن بعضهم ينطق بذلك ويقول: (أما بعد) كما وبعضهم يحذف (أما) ويأتي بدلها بالواو فيقول: (وبعد) كما هنا؛ فالواو نائبةُ النائب هو السنة. وهل الظرف من معمولات الشرط أو من معمولات الجزاء؟ خلافٌ، والراجح كونه من معمولات الجزاء؛ ليكون المعلق عليه مطلقاً، وهو أبلغ في التحقيق؛ لأن المعنى عليه: إن وُجد شيءٌ في الدنيا مطلقاً، فأقول بعد ... إلى آخره، ولا يرد أن الفاءَ لا يعملُ ما بعدها فيما قبلها؛ لتوسعهم في الظروف. و (بعد) ظرف زمان كثيراً، ومكان قليلاً، وهي هنا صالحة للزمان باعتبار النطق، وللمكان باعتبار الرَّقْم. واختلف فى أول مَنْ نطق بها على أقوال: أقربها: أنه داود، وكانت له فصل الخطاب؛ أي: يفصلُ بها بين الحقِّ والباطل. وقيل: يُفصلُ بها بين نوع من الكلام ونوع آخر منه.
After; knowledge of the foundation of religion is incumbent, needing for the prophets. His statement: (Wa ba'du) is built on damma due to deletion of the added-to and intention of its meaning. The estimation: after the basmala, the hamdala, and the prayer and peace upon the Prophet, his family, companions, and party. It is possible that it is in the accusative without tanwin due to deletion of the added-to and intention of its word. But the well-known on tongues is the first. It is a word brought for transition from one style to another style: i.e., from one type of speech to another type. The type transferred from is the basmala and what follows it; the type transferred to is the explanation of the reason impelling authorship. Its second origin is (amma ba'du), evidenced by the necessary occurrence of fa in its scope mostly. This origin is the sunna, for he (the Prophet) used to bring it in his sermons and letters. It is established that he gave a sermon saying “Amma ba'du”. The ultimate origin is “mahma yakun min shay'in ba'du”; mahma is a conditional noun as subject, yakun is the conditional verb, which is the complete kan, and its subject is an implicit pronoun returning to mahma. “Min shay'in” is specification of mahma, although specification normally specifies, it may be equal indicating that the intention is the entire genus. Then mahma, yakun, and min shay'in were elided and (amma) was set in their place. Then some people pronounce that and say (amma ba'du), and some delete amma and bring in its place waw, saying (wa ba'du) as here. So the waw is the deputy of the deputy, which is the sunna. Is the adverb from the operands of the condition or from the operands of the result? There is disagreement, and the preponderant is that it is from the operands of the result, so that what is suspended upon is absolute, and that is more effective in verification, because the meaning is: if something exists in the world absolutely, then I say after... to the end. It is not objected that the fa does not operate what is after it on what is before it, due to their leniency in adverbs. And (ba'du) is an adverb of time often, and of place rarely, and it is here suitable for time in terms of utterance, and for place in terms of writing. And there is disagreement concerning the first who uttered it, with sayings: the nearest is that it was Dawud, and he had the decisive speech, i.e., he distinguishes between truth and falsehood by it. And it is said he distinguishes between one type of speech and another type.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and rhetorical commentary on the phrase 'wa ba'du' that begins the poem. The commentator first clarifies the construction: 'wa ba'du' is built on damma because the added noun is omitted and the meaning is intended. The implied meaning is 'after the basmala, hamdala, and salawat'. Alternatively, it could be in the accusative without tanwin if the word itself is intended, but the common usage is the first. The phrase functions as a transition marker from one style to another, specifically from the introductory invocations to the statement of the reason for writing the book. The commentator then traces its origin: the original form is 'amma ba'du', which the Prophet (ﷺ) used in his sermons and letters, so it is Sunnah. The underlying structure is a conditional sentence: 'mahma yakun min shay'in ba'du' (whatever thing exists after), with 'mahma' as the subject, 'yakun' as the verb, and 'min shay'in' as a specification. The words 'mahma', 'yakun', and 'min shay'in' were omitted and replaced by 'amma'. Some people use 'amma ba'du', while others replace 'amma' with 'waw' and say 'wa ba'du', as here. The waw thus stands in for 'amma', which itself replaced the original conditional phrase; hence the waw is a 'deputy of a deputy'. There is a discussion about whether the adverb 'ba'du' belongs to the conditional clause or to the result clause; the preferred view is that it belongs to the result, making the condition absolute ('if anything exists at all, then I say after...'). The word 'ba'du' is primarily a time adverb but can be a place adverb; here it is suitable for both in different senses. Finally, the commentator notes the disagreement about the first person to utter this phrase; the strongest opinion is that it was Prophet Dawud (David), who used it as a decisive distinction between truth and falsehood, while others say it distinguishes between types of speech.

Key Terms
وبعد
البناء على الضم
حذف المضاف إليه

Origin of 'Wa Ba'du' from the Sunnah and Conditional Sentence
Tracing the origin of 'wa ba'du' through prophetic usage and conditional sentences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical and linguistic origin of 'wa ba'du' from the Prophetic phrase 'amma ba'du' and an underlying conditional construction.

Core Idea
'Wa ba'du' derives from the Prophet's sunnah phrase 'amma ba'du', which itself comes from a conditional sentence.

This lesson traces the lineage of 'wa ba'du' back to its roots. The commentator shows it is not a random expression but a shortened form of 'amma ba'du', which the Prophet himself used, and ultimately a conditional sentence 'whatever thing exists after...' . We also explore the debate about the grammatical role of the adverb 'ba'du' and the identity of the first user.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the origin of 'wa ba'du'
Read from 'وأصلها الثاني : (أما بعد)' to the end. Focus on the chain: 'amma ba'du' -> 'mahma yakun min shay'in ba'du'. Understand why 'wa ba'du' is called 'deputy of a deputy'.
The phrase 'نائبة النائب' means the waw is a substitute for 'amma', which itself substituted for the conditional phrase.
2
Understand the conditional sentence breakdown
The commentator gives the full conditional: 'مهما يكن من شيء بعد'. Identify the parts: 'مهما' as subject, 'يكن' as verb, 'من شيء' as specification. See how they were elided.
This shows the depth of Arabic grammatical analysis.
3
Consider the disagreement about the adverb
Read the part about whether 'بعد' is an operand of the condition or the result. Note the preferred view and its logical consequence.
4
Learn about the first user
Read the final sentences about the first person who said it. Note the strongest opinion is Dawud (David).
This adds a historical dimension.

وَبَعْدُ فَالعِلْمُ بأَصْلِ الدِّينِ مُحَنَّمٌ يَحْتَاجُ لِلنَّ قوله: (وَبَعْدُ) بالبناء على الضم لحذف المضاف إليه ونيَّة معناه، والتقدير : وبعد البسملة والحمدلة والصلاة والسلام على النبي وآله وصحبه وحزبه، ويحتمل أن يكونَ بالنصب من غير تنوين لحذف المضاف إليه ونية لفظه، لكن المشهور على الألسنة الأول، وهي كلمة يُؤتى بها للانتقال من أسلوب إلى أسلوب آخر؛ أي: من نوع من الكلام إلى نوع آخر، والنوع المنتقلُ منه هو البسملة وما بعدها، والمنتقل إليه هو بيان السبب الحامل على التأليف. وأصلها الثاني : (أما بعد) ؛ بدليل لزوم الفاء في حيّزها غالباً، وهذا الأصل هو السنة؛ فقد كان يأتي بها في خطبه ومراسلاته؛ وصح أنه خطب فقال: «أما بعد». والأصل الأصيل مهما يكن مِنْ شيءٍ بعد؛ فمهما: اسم شرط مبتدأ، ويكن فعل الشرط، وهو مضارعُ (كانَ) التامَّةِ ، وفاعله ضمير مستتر تقديره هو يعود على (مهما) و (من شيء) بيان لمهما وإن كان شأن البيان التخصيص؛ فقد يكونُ مساوياً إشارة إلى أن المراد الجنس بتمامه، فحذفت (مهما) و(يكن) و(من شيء)، وأقيمت (أما ) مقام ذلك، ثم إن بعضهم ينطق بذلك ويقول: (أما بعد) كما وبعضهم يحذف (أما) ويأتي بدلها بالواو فيقول: (وبعد) كما هنا؛ فالواو نائبةُ النائب هو السنة. وهل الظرف من معمولات الشرط أو من معمولات الجزاء؟ خلافٌ، والراجح كونه من معمولات الجزاء؛ ليكون المعلق عليه مطلقاً، وهو أبلغ في التحقيق؛ لأن المعنى عليه: إن وُجد شيءٌ في الدنيا مطلقاً، فأقول بعد ... إلى آخره، ولا يرد أن الفاءَ لا يعملُ ما بعدها فيما قبلها؛ لتوسعهم في الظروف. و (بعد) ظرف زمان كثيراً، ومكان قليلاً، وهي هنا صالحة للزمان باعتبار النطق، وللمكان باعتبار الرَّقْم. واختلف فى أول مَنْ نطق بها على أقوال: أقربها: أنه داود، وكانت له فصل الخطاب؛ أي: يفصلُ بها بين الحقِّ والباطل. وقيل: يُفصلُ بها بين نوع من الكلام ونوع آخر منه.
After; knowledge of the foundation of religion is incumbent, needing for the prophets. His statement: (Wa ba'du) is built on damma due to deletion of the added-to and intention of its meaning. The estimation: after the basmala, the hamdala, and the prayer and peace upon the Prophet, his family, companions, and party. It is possible that it is in the accusative without tanwin due to deletion of the added-to and intention of its word. But the well-known on tongues is the first. It is a word brought for transition from one style to another style: i.e., from one type of speech to another type. The type transferred from is the basmala and what follows it; the type transferred to is the explanation of the reason impelling authorship. Its second origin is (amma ba'du), evidenced by the necessary occurrence of fa in its scope mostly. This origin is the sunna, for he (the Prophet) used to bring it in his sermons and letters. It is established that he gave a sermon saying “Amma ba'du”. The ultimate origin is “mahma yakun min shay'in ba'du”; mahma is a conditional noun as subject, yakun is the conditional verb, which is the complete kan, and its subject is an implicit pronoun returning to mahma. “Min shay'in” is specification of mahma, although specification normally specifies, it may be equal indicating that the intention is the entire genus. Then mahma, yakun, and min shay'in were elided and (amma) was set in their place. Then some people pronounce that and say (amma ba'du), and some delete amma and bring in its place waw, saying (wa ba'du) as here. So the waw is the deputy of the deputy, which is the sunna. Is the adverb from the operands of the condition or from the operands of the result? There is disagreement, and the preponderant is that it is from the operands of the result, so that what is suspended upon is absolute, and that is more effective in verification, because the meaning is: if something exists in the world absolutely, then I say after... to the end. It is not objected that the fa does not operate what is after it on what is before it, due to their leniency in adverbs. And (ba'du) is an adverb of time often, and of place rarely, and it is here suitable for time in terms of utterance, and for place in terms of writing. And there is disagreement concerning the first who uttered it, with sayings: the nearest is that it was Dawud, and he had the decisive speech, i.e., he distinguishes between truth and falsehood by it. And it is said he distinguishes between one type of speech and another type.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and rhetorical commentary on the phrase 'wa ba'du' that begins the poem. The commentator first clarifies the construction: 'wa ba'du' is built on damma because the added noun is omitted and the meaning is intended. The implied meaning is 'after the basmala, hamdala, and salawat'. Alternatively, it could be in the accusative without tanwin if the word itself is intended, but the common usage is the first. The phrase functions as a transition marker from one style to another, specifically from the introductory invocations to the statement of the reason for writing the book. The commentator then traces its origin: the original form is 'amma ba'du', which the Prophet (ﷺ) used in his sermons and letters, so it is Sunnah. The underlying structure is a conditional sentence: 'mahma yakun min shay'in ba'du' (whatever thing exists after), with 'mahma' as the subject, 'yakun' as the verb, and 'min shay'in' as a specification. The words 'mahma', 'yakun', and 'min shay'in' were omitted and replaced by 'amma'. Some people use 'amma ba'du', while others replace 'amma' with 'waw' and say 'wa ba'du', as here. The waw thus stands in for 'amma', which itself replaced the original conditional phrase; hence the waw is a 'deputy of a deputy'. There is a discussion about whether the adverb 'ba'du' belongs to the conditional clause or to the result clause; the preferred view is that it belongs to the result, making the condition absolute ('if anything exists at all, then I say after...'). The word 'ba'du' is primarily a time adverb but can be a place adverb; here it is suitable for both in different senses. Finally, the commentator notes the disagreement about the first person to utter this phrase; the strongest opinion is that it was Prophet Dawud (David), who used it as a decisive distinction between truth and falsehood, while others say it distinguishes between types of speech.

Key Terms

مهما
فصل الخطاب
نائبة النائب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'wa ba'du' is an original expression unrelated to the Prophet.
Correction: The commentator shows it is directly derived from the Prophet's sunnah and ultimately from a conditional construction.

Checkpoint

D) The statement of a companion

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'wa ba'du' has a rich historical and grammatical lineage, ultimately rooted in a conditional sentence and the Prophet's sunnah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you use the phrase 'wa ba'du' in your writing or speech, do you consciously connect to the Prophet's sunnah and the deep linguistic tradition behind it, or is it just a habit? What would change in your heart if you truly felt you were continuing a prophetic practice?
?
The phrase 'amma ba'du' was used by Dawud to separate truth from falsehood. In your daily conversations, when you transition between topics, do you ever pause to ensure you are distinguishing truth from falsehood? How can you make your speech more 'fasl al-khitab'?
?
The derivation shows that even common phrases have profound origins. What does this teach you about the weight of your words? Are there other phrases you use carelessly that might have deep roots in revelation or sunnah? Commit to researching one such phrase this week.
