The Correct Opinion and Its Universal Scope
Sanusi declares the third opinion correct and expands the scope of the disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the correct opinion is the third one and how the disagreement about taqlid applies universally to all types of theological reasoning and to all people.

Core Idea
The disagreement about taqlid is general—it covers all theological knowledge and all people.

Having presented six opinions on taqlid, Sanusi now declares which one is correct and expands its scope. He affirms the third opinion and clarifies that the dispute applies not only to knowledge of God but to all types of nazar, and not only to isolated individuals but to everyone. This lesson establishes the universality of the obligation of nazar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the correct opinion
Read the passage and note that the author explicitly states the third opinion is the correct one. This is the foundation for the rest of the passage.
The third opinion is the one that says taqlid is a sin only if one is capable of nazar.
2
See the first expansion: all types of nazar
The passage then states that the disagreement is general (muṭlaq), applying to nazar leading to knowledge of God and to other types, such as knowledge of the messengers. This refutes those who restrict it.
3
See the second expansion: all people
Finally, the author expands the disagreement to include all people, whether from cities or remote mountains, contrary to those who restrict it to the latter.
Shaykh ʿAbd al-Salām is cited as holding both restrictive views, but the author rejects them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقولُ الحقُّ الذي عليه المعوَّلُ من هذه الأقوال : القول الثالث، والصواب أن هذا الخلاف مطلق؛ أي جارٍ في النظر الموصل لمعرفة الله تعالى وفي غيره؛ كالنظر الموصل لمعرفة الرسل، خلافاً لمن خصَّ الخلاف بالنظر غير الموصل لمعرفة الله تعالى، وقال: أما النظر الموصل لمعرفة الله تعالى فهو واجب بالإجماع، وقد جرى على ذلك الشيخ عبد السلام والراجح أنه لا فرق في هذا الخلاف بين أهل الأمصار والقرى، وبين من نشأ في شاهق جبل، خلافاً لمن خصّه بمن نشأ في شاهق جبل دون أهل الأمصار والقرى وقد جرى على ذلك الشيخ عبد السلام أيضاً
And the true saying upon which reliance is placed among these opinions is the third opinion, and the correct view is that this disagreement is general; i.e., it applies to nazar leading to knowledge of God Almighty and to other types, such as nazar leading to knowledge of the messengers, contrary to those who restricted the disagreement to nazar not leading to knowledge of God Almighty and said: 'As for nazar leading to knowledge of God Almighty, it is obligatory by consensus.' And Shaykh Abd al-Salam held this. And the preponderant view is that there is no difference in this disagreement between the people of cities and villages, and between one who grew up in a remote mountain peak, contrary to those who restricted it to one who grew up in a remote mountain peak excluding the people of cities and villages, and Shaykh Abd al-Salam also held this.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage identifies the correct opinion among the six as the third opinion (taqlid with sin if capable of nazar, otherwise no sin). It then clarifies that the disagreement about taqlid is general (mutlaq): it applies to all types of nazar, whether leading to knowledge of God or to knowledge of the messengers, contrary to those who restrict it to nazar that does not lead to knowledge of God. The author notes that Shaykh Abd al-Salam held that view. Furthermore, the disagreement applies equally to people of cities and villages and to those raised in remote mountain peaks, contrary to those who restrict it to the latter. Again, Shaykh Abd al-Salam held this. The author thus expands the scope of the dispute, indicating that the issue is not limited to a particular context.

Key Terms
نظر موصل (nazar leading to knowledge)
خلاف مطلق (general disagreement)
أهل الأمصار (people of cities)
شاهق جبل (remote mountain peak)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the disagreement about taqlid only concerns the knowledge of God, but it actually applies to all theological knowledge.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the disagreement is general and includes nazar for knowing the messengers as well.

Checkpoint
According to the correct opinion in this passage, to what does the disagreement about taqlid apply?
A) Only to nazar leading to knowledge of God
B) Only to nazar not leading to knowledge of God
C) To all types of nazar, including knowledge of the messengers
D) Only to those raised in remote mountains

Key Takeaway
The correct opinion on taqlid is the third, and the disagreement is universal in subject and person.

Defense of Kalam and Common Ignorance
The author quotes a poem defending kalam and relates a story illustrating commoners' theological errors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the study of kalam against critics and illustrates the dangerous ignorance common among people in theological matters.

Core Idea
Kalam is like the sun: its truth shines regardless of critics blinded by ignorance.

This lesson presents a poetic defense of kalam and a telling anecdote that reveals the theological confusion rampant among ordinary people. The author uses these examples to underscore the necessity of sound theological study and to refute those who dismiss it.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem and translation
Read the Arabic poem and its English translation. Note the metaphor of the sun and the blind: the sun's light is not diminished by the inability of the blind to see it.
The poem is in basit meter, a common Arabic poetic meter.
2
Reflect on the anecdote
Read the story of the two women. Identify the theological error in the second woman's statement. She says 'He will forgive us if God wills, whom He Himself created also' – this implies that God is created, which is shirk.
Al-Yusi heard this in his youth, showing even seemingly religious people can err.
3
Consider the author's conclusion
The author lists other common errors: believing the Companions are prophets (kufr) and denying the Resurrection. Ask: How do these examples support the need for kalam?
These examples show that without proper theology, people fall into clear unbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما أحسن قول بعضهم : [من البسيط]
عَابَ الكَلامَ أُناسٌ لا خَلَاقَ لَهُمْ وَما عَلَيْهِ إذا هابُوهُ مِنْ ضَرَرٍ
ما ضَرَّ شَمْسَ الضُّحَى فِي الأُفْقِ طَالِعَةٌ أَلَّا يَرَى ضَوْءَهَا مَنْ لَيْسَ ذَا بَصَرِ
قال اليوسي : وقد تحدثت امرأتان بمحضري في زمن صغري، وذكرتا الذنوب، فقالت إحداهما : الله يغفر لنا، فقالت الأخرى : يغفر لنا إن وفَقَهُ اللهُ الذي خلقه هو أيضاً . انتهى (٤) ، ومثل ذلك كثير في الناس؛ فمنهم من يعتقد أن الصحابة أنبياء، وهذا كفر، ومنهم من ينكر البعث ويقول : من مات ثم جاء وأخبر بذلك؟! إلى غير ذلك من الكفر الصريح .
And how excellent is the saying of some of them: [in basit meter]
People devoid of goodness have criticized kalam (theology), and no harm comes to it if they fear it.
What harm does the forenoon sun rising in the horizon suffer if one who has no sight does not see its light?
Al-Yusi said: Two women spoke in my presence in my youth, and they mentioned sins. One of them said: 'God will forgive us.' The other said: 'He will forgive us if God wills, whom He Himself created also.' End (4). And similar things are many among the people; some of them believe that the Companions are prophets, which is unbelief, and some deny the Resurrection and say: 'Who died and came and informed about that?' and so on from clear unbelief.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage defends the study of kalam (theology) against its critics. The author cites a poetic verse: people who lack goodness criticize kalam, but it is unharmed by their rejection, just as the sun is unharmed by the blind not seeing its light. This metaphor suggests that kalam's value is intrinsic, and criticism arises from spiritual blindness. Then the author relates an anecdote from al-Yusi about two women: one said 'God will forgive us,' and the other absurdly responded 'He will forgive us if God wills, whom He Himself created also'—a confused statement implying that God is created. The author notes that such ignorance is common: some believe the Companions are prophets (kufr), others deny bodily resurrection, etc. This illustrates the necessity of proper theological knowledge and refutes those who dismiss kalam as unnecessary.

Key Terms
كلام (kalam)
بصير (sight/blindness)
صحابة (Companions)
بعث (Resurrection)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Kalam (theology) is harmful or unnecessary and should be avoided.
Correction: The poem shows that kalam is like the sun: its benefit remains even if critics (the blind) cannot see it. The anecdote further shows that without kalam, people fall into grave errors like believing God is created or denying the Resurrection.

Checkpoint
What does the anecdote from al-Yusi illustrate about common people?
A) That they are all believers
B) That they hold confused and heretical beliefs
C) That they follow the Sunnah correctly
D) That they should not study kalam

Key Takeaway
The study of kalam is essential because even seemingly pious people can fall into grave theological errors without proper knowledge.

Knowledge as the First Duty, Not a Pillar
Clarifies that knowledge is the first duty but not a pillar of faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge is the first duty for every accountable person yet is not considered a foundational pillar of religion.

Core Idea
Knowledge is the first duty, but not a pillar—taqlid is sufficient.

This lesson unpacks a key theological nuance: even though knowledge of Allah is the very first obligation a person has, it is not a pillar of faith that denial of it would constitute disbelief. The passage explains the command to be certain about this ranking and clarifies that the soundest view permits taqlid. We'll follow the author's careful distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Notice the key terms: اجزم (be certain), أول ما يجب (first what is obligatory), معرفة (knowledge), and خلف منتصب (established disagreement).
The passage combines a verse from the poem with a commentary by the author.
2
Identify the addressees of the command
The commentary specifies that the command to be certain applies to every mukallaf—male, female, free, slave, jinn, human. Ask yourself why this universal scope matters.
This emphasizes that the duty of knowledge is universal.
3
Distinguish the two claims about knowledge
The author distinguishes between 'knowledge is obligatory' and 'knowledge is the first obligation'. The previous line already established the former; this passage adds the latter. Then immediately he says this issue is not a pillar of religion.
The phrase 'هذه المسألة ليست من أركان الدين' is crucial.
4
Understand why it's not a pillar
The reason given: 'because the soundest view is the sufficiency of taqlid'. If someone can be a believer by blindly following, then the obligation of knowledge is not such that violation amounts to kufr.
This guards against the misconception that denying knowledge as first duty is apostasy.
5
Reflect on the grammatical note
The passage ends with a brief morphology of 'awwal'. This is a reminder that theological reasoning also pays attention to language. Notice how the author integrates grammar into the explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاجْزِمْ بِأَنَّ أَوَّلاً مِمَّا يَجِبْ مَعْرِفَةٌ وَفِيهِ خُلْفٌ مُنْتَصِبـ وقوله : (وَاجْزِمْ) أي: اعتقد اعتقاداً جازماً، والمخاطب بذلك كل مكلف من ذكرٍ أو أنثى، حرّ أو عبد جنيّ أو إنسي، قال المصنف في شرحه: (والكلام السابق من قوله: فكل من كلف ... إلى آخره، إنما أفاد أن المعرفة واجبة على المكلَّف، وهذا أفاد أنها أوَّلُ واجب، ثم هذه المسألة ليست من أركان الدين المعتقدة، كيف والأصح كفاية التقليد؟! . وقوله : (بِأَنَّ أَوَّلاً) متعلق بـ (اجزم) ، وأصل (أول): أَوْأَل على وزن (أَفْعَل) قلبت الهمزة الثانية واواً ، ثم أدغمت الواو في الواو لاجتماع المثلين، وله استعمالان .
And be certain that the first of what is obligatory is knowledge, and concerning it there is an established disagreement. And his statement: (wajzim) meaning: believe with a certain belief. The addressee in that is every accountable person, male or female, free or slave, jinn or human. The author said in his commentary: 'The previous discourse from his statement: "For everyone who is accountable..." up to its end only indicated that knowledge is obligatory upon the accountable person, and this indicates that it is the first obligation. Then this issue is not among the foundational pillars of religion, for how [could it be] while the soundest view is the sufficiency of taqlid?' And his statement: (bi anna awwalan) is connected to (wa jzim). The origin of (awwal) is aw'al on the pattern of af'al, the second hamza was changed to waw, then the waw was assimilated into the waw for the meeting of two similar letters, and it has two usages.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage explains the verse 'Wajzim bi anna awwalan mimma yajib ma'rifa wa fihi khulfun muntasib'. The commentator first glosses 'wajzim' as 'believe with certainty' (i'tiqad jazim), and notes that the command is addressed to every accountable person (mukallaf), male or female, free or slave, jinn or human. He then quotes the author of the original poem: the earlier statement that knowledge is obligatory on the mukallaf is now specified as being the first obligation (awwal wajib). However, the author immediately qualifies this: 'this issue is not among the foundational pillars (arkan al-din al-mu'taqada)'. Why? Because the soundest view (al-asahh) is that taqlid suffices—meaning a person can be a believer without personally acquiring knowledge through reflection. Thus, the obligation of knowledge is not a matter of such certainty that denying it would constitute disbelief. The commentator then explains the grammatical parsing: 'bi anna awwalan' is attached to 'wajzim', and he provides a brief morphology lesson: the word 'awwal' originates from 'aw'al' (on the pattern af'al), where the second hamza is converted to waw, then assimilated due to identical letters. He notes that 'awwal' has two usages (as a superlative or as a noun). The purpose of the passage is to clarify that while ma'rifa is the first duty, it is not an article of faith, and disagreement about it is permissible—hence the phrase 'wa fihi khulfun muntasib' (an established disagreement exists about it).

Key Terms
معرفة
أول واجب
أركان الدين
مكلف
تقليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if knowledge is the first obligation, then denying it necessarily makes one a disbeliever.
Correction: This passage clarifies that knowledge as the first duty is not a pillar of faith, and the soundest view holds that taqlid suffices, so disagreement about it does not constitute kufr.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, is the obligation of knowledge as the first duty considered one of the foundational pillars of religion?
A) Yes, it is an essential pillar.
B) No, because the soundest view is that taqlid suffices, so it is not a pillar.
C) Yes, because denial of it constitutes kufr.
D) No, because knowledge is not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is the first duty for every accountable person, but it is not a pillar of faith because the soundest view is that taqlid suffices.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You have just learned that knowledge is the first duty placed upon you, yet you are told that taqlid is sufficient. Will you commit to learning at least one thing about your faith with certainty this week?
?
The commentator says the soundest view is that taqlid suffices—but does that excuse you from the command to be certain (wajzim)? If you have not prioritized learning the basics of your faith, what does that say about your response to Allah's command? When will you take a concrete step beyond blind following?
?
This lesson draws a line: denying that knowledge is the first obligation is not kufr because it is not a pillar. But you have been commanded to be certain. Have you ever honestly examined your own beliefs, or have you assumed they are correct? What is one doubt you've buried because answering it would be uncomfortable? Will you face it today and seek the truth?

Clarifying the Disagreement on the First Obligation
Commentary on the line 'And regarding it there is a standing disagreement'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Muslim scholarly disagreement about the 'first obligation' concerns only the priority between knowledge of Allah and the reflection leading to it, not the obligation itself.

Core Idea
The disagreement is about priority, not obligation.

This passage addresses a standing disagreement among Muslims regarding the first duty upon a responsible person. Some say it is knowledge of Allah, others say it is the reflection that leads to knowledge. The commentator clarifies that this dispute is only about which act is first in order or merit, not about whether either act is obligatory. Indeed, all agree that knowing Allah and performing reflection to that end are obligatory within human capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the footnote numbers and how they provide additional clarity.
2
Identify the two positions mentioned
The passage mentions previous sayings that reflection is forbidden or a condition of perfection. Note these as background views that the commentator is addressing.
The commentator seems to set these aside, focusing on the mainstream view.
3
Understand the key clarification
The passage and footnote explicitly state that the disagreement is about priority (al-awwaliyya), not about obligation (al-wujūb). This is the core point.
Remember: all Muslims agree on obligation; they differ on which comes first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خلاف (1) بين المسلمين في وجوب المعرفة ووجوب النظر الموصل إليها، كذا قال الشارح ) ، لكن قد سبق قول بحرمة النظر ، وقول بأنه شرط كمال، وكأنه ناظر لما جرى عليه فيما تقدم من تخصيص الخلاف بغير معرفة الله تعالى وغير النظر الموصل إليها، وقد تقدَّم ما فيه، ويحتمل أنه لم يعتد بالخلاف، بناء على ما أنشده السيوطي في «الإتقان (۲): وَلَيْسَ كُلُّ خِلافٍ جَاءَ مُعْتَبَراً إِلَّا خِلافٌ لَهُ حَظِّ مِنَ النَّظَرِ

(1) كذا في شرح ابن الناظم على الجوهرة (ص .(٤٦) . وانظر هداية المريده للناظم (٢١٥/١) وعبارته : لم يقع خلاف بين المسلمين في وجوب معرفة الله سبحانه وتعالى، ولا في وجوب النظر الموصل إليها بقدر الطاقة البشرية؛ كما قال السعد وغيره كما مر، وهذا يُفهم من جعله الخلاف في الأولية دون الوجوب).

(۲) أورده في الإتقان» (٤٥/١) ضمن أبيات للحصار في .
There is disagreement (1) among Muslims regarding the obligation of knowledge and the obligation of the reflection (naẓar) that leads to it – so said the commentator. But previously there occurred a saying that reflection is forbidden, and a saying that it is a condition of perfection, and it seems he is looking at what preceded regarding the specification of disagreement to [matters] other than knowledge of Allah, may He be exalted, and other than the reflection that leads to it, and what is in it has already preceded. And it is possible that he did not consider the disagreement valid, based on what al-Suyūṭī recited in al-Itqān (2): 'And not every disagreement that comes is considered, except a disagreement that has a share of consideration.'

(1) Thus in the commentary of Ibn al-Nāẓim on al-Jawhara (p. 46). And look at Hidāyat al-Murīd of the author (1/215), and his statement: 'There did not occur disagreement among Muslims regarding the obligation of knowing Allah, may He be exalted and high, nor regarding the obligation of the reflection that leads to it to the extent of human capacity – as al-Sa‘d and others said, as has passed – and this is understood from his making the disagreement about priority, not about obligation.'

(2) He cited it in al-Itqān (1/45) among the verses of al-Ḥiṣār.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the poet's line 'And regarding it there is a standing disagreement'. The commentator explains that the disagreement (khilāf) concerns whether the first obligatory act for a person is knowledge (ma‘rifa) of Allah or the reflection (naẓar) that leads to that knowledge. He notes that some scholars have said reflection is forbidden, others that it is a condition of perfection (i.e., supererogatory), but the author of the commentary here seems to restrict the disagreement to matters other than the knowledge of Allah and the reflection that leads to it, implying that the obligation of knowledge itself is agreed upon. The commentator then suggests that the poet may not have considered the disagreement valid, citing a line from al-Suyūṭī: 'Not every disagreement is considered, except one that has a share of consideration.' The footnote (1) clarifies that the disagreement is about priority (al-awwaliyya), not about the obligation (al-wujūb) itself, meaning that all Muslims agree that knowing Allah and performing the reflection that leads to it are obligatory, but they differ on which comes first temporally or in priority. The footnote (2) gives the source of the cited line.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The disagreement among Muslims is about whether knowing Allah is obligatory at all.
Correction: The footnote clarifies that Muslims agree on the obligation of knowing Allah; the disagreement is only about which act (knowledge or reflection) is first in priority.

Checkpoint

D) Whether the first obligation is knowledge of Allah's essence or His attributes

Key Takeaway
The Muslim scholarly disagreement on the 'first obligation' concerns only priority, not obligation; all agree that knowing Allah and the reflection leading to it are obligatory.

The lesson emphasizes that knowing Allah is an obligation. Are you actively seeking that knowledge, or have you been distracted by secondary matters? What concrete step will you take today to prioritize gaining knowledge of Allah?

Three Opinions on the First Obligation
Enumerating the first three of twelve positions on the first obligatory act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first three scholarly opinions on what constitutes the first obligatory act for a Muslim, and the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
Scholars disagreed on the very first duty: knowledge, reflection, or its first premise.

In Sunni theology, the question of the very first obligation (أول الواجبات) is foundational. Different schools of thought have offered different answers, each with a distinct emphasis on the role of knowledge, reasoning, and faith. This passage presents the first three of twelve recorded opinions, beginning with the views of Imam al-Ash‘arī, Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarā’īnī, and al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and its literal translation to get the author's enumeration of the first three opinions.
Notice the structured presentation with 'first', 'second', 'third'.
2
Identify each opinion and its proponent
Clearly note who said what: al-Ash‘arī (knowledge), al-Isfarā’īnī (reflection), al-Bāqillānī (beginning of reflection).
The second opinion is also attributed to al-Ash‘arī.
3
Examine the footnotes
Footnotes clarify the definition of knowledge as faith, and later scholars' preferences. See how al-Sanūsī and al-Ḥāmidī engaged with these views.
Note the reference to al-Ghazālī and Ibn Kullāb.
4
Reflect on the diversity
Consider why such a fundamental question has different answers. The differences reflect varying emphases on the means versus the end of religious obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجملة الأقوال في أول الواجبات اثنا عشر قولاً (۳) :
أولها : ما قاله الأشعري إمامُ هذا الفن : أنه المعرفةُ (٤) . وثانيها : ما قاله الأستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفرايني : أنه النظر الموصل للمعرفة، ويُعزى للأشعري أيضاً (٥) . وثالثها : ما قاله القاضي الباقلاني : أنه أوّلُ النظر ؛ أي: المقدمة الأولى منه؛ نحو قولك : العالم حادث، وكل حادث لا بدَّ له من محدث؛ فمجموع المقدمتين هو النظر، والمقدمة الأولى هي أوَّلُ النظر .

(۳) وهي من جمع العلامة اليوسي كما حكاه العلامة الأمير في حاشيته) (ص ٤٧)، وانظر شرح الكبرى للسنوسي (ص (۲۷).
(٤) هذه المعرفة تمامها بتمام الإيمان الشرعي، وهي التي قال عنها حجة الإسلام إمامنا الغزالي في إحيائه» (٣١٤/٤) : (أصل السعادات هي المعرفةُ التي عبر الشرعُ عنها بالإيمان)، وهي مخلوقةٌ مكتسبة، قال الإمام ابن كلاب كما نقله الحافظ الخركوشي في تهذيب الأسرار» (ص ٤٣): (المعرفة فعل الإنسان، وهي مخلوقة الله عز وجل).
(٥) واختاره الإمام السنوسي في شرح الكبرى (ص) (۲۸) وعلل ذلك بقوله : (التكرر الحث على النظر في الكتاب والسنة، حتى كأنه مقصد)، وعلّق عليه العلامة الحامدي بقوله : (قد يقال: إن المعرفة مقصد حقيقة، فالأولى اختيار القول بأنها أول واجب).
The total of the statements regarding the first of the obligations is twelve statements (3): The first of them: what al-Ash‘arī, the imam of this art, said: that it is knowledge (ma‘rifa) (4). The second: what the master Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarā’īnī said: that it is the reflection (naẓar) that leads to knowledge, and it is also attributed to al-Ash‘arī (5). The third: what al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī said: that it is the beginning of reflection (awwal al-naẓar), i.e. the first premise thereof, like your saying: 'The world is originated, and every originated thing necessarily has an originator'; so the sum of the two premises is the reflection, and the first premise is the beginning of the reflection.

(3) And they are from the collection of the scholar al-Yūsī, as related by the scholar al-Amīr in his gloss (p. 47), and see Sharḥ al-Kubrā of al-Sanūsī (p. 27).
(4) This knowledge in its completion is the completion of legal faith (īmān), and it is that about which the Proof of Islam, our imam al-Ghazālī, said in his Iḥyā’ (4/314): 'The root of happiness is the knowledge that the Law has expressed as faith.' And it is created and acquired; Imam Ibn Kullāb said, as transmitted by al-Ḥāfiẓ al-Kharkūshī in Tahdhīb al-Asrār (p. 43): 'Knowledge is an act of the human being, and it is created by Allah, mighty and majestic.'
(5) And al-Sanūsī chose it in Sharḥ al-Kubrā (p. 28) and gave as reason: 'The frequent exhortation to reflection in the Book and the Sunna, such that it seems like a purpose.' And the scholar al-Ḥāmidī commented on it saying: 'It may be said that knowledge is the true purpose, so it is preferable to choose the statement that it is the first obligation.'
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the opinions on what is the first obligatory act for a Muslim, introduced by the phrase 'Twelve statements.' The author presents the first three opinions, which are from major Sunni theologians. The first opinion is that of Imam al-Ash‘arī: the first obligation is knowledge (ma‘rifa). The footnote (4) explains that this knowledge is identified with legal faith (īmān) and quotes al-Ghazālī calling it 'the root of happiness'; it also notes that this knowledge is created and acquired, citing Ibn Kullāb. The second opinion is that of Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarā’īnī: the first obligation is the reflection (naẓar) that leads to knowledge, and this is also attributed to al-Ash‘arī. The footnote (5) mentions that al-Sanūsī preferred this view, arguing that the frequent commands to reflect in the Qur’an and Sunna make reflection itself the primary duty, although al-Ḥāmidī noted that since knowledge is the ultimate goal, one might still say knowledge is the first obligation. The third opinion is that of al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī: the first obligation is the beginning of reflection (awwal al-naẓar), i.e., the first premise of the argument for God's existence, such as 'the world is originated.' The commentary then lists the remaining nine opinions in other parts of the book. The footnote (3) notes that these twelve opinions were collected by al-Yūsī.

Key Terms
أول الواجبات
المعرفة
النظر
أول النظر
الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first obligation for a Muslim is knowledge of Allah without any prior reasoning.
Correction: While al-Ash‘arī held that knowledge is first, other major theologians like al-Isfarā’īnī and al-Bāqillānī argued that reflection or its first premise is actually the primary duty.

Checkpoint
According to al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī, what is the first obligation?
A) Knowledge of Allah (ma‘rifa)
B) The reflection that leads to knowledge (naẓar)
C) The first premise of reflection (awwal al-naẓar)
D) Faith (īmān)

Key Takeaway
The first three opinions on the first obligation show a spectrum from the end (knowledge) to the means (reflection) to the starting point (first premise).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage notes that some say reflection is the first duty because the Quran repeatedly exhorts it. When was the last time you truly reflected on the createdness of the world? How would that change your daily awareness of God?
?
Al-Ash'ari said the first obligation is knowledge, but the footnote describes this knowledge as the root of happiness. Do you experience your knowledge of God as the root of your happiness? If not, what is missing?

The Order of Contemplation: Self, Upper World, Lower World
Commentator explains the commanded order of looking to oneself, then upper world, then lower world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the prescribed order of contemplation as a means to knowledge of God, starting with oneself, then the upper world, then the lower world.

Core Idea
Contemplation of creation follows a divinely guided order: self, heavens, earth.

In this passage, the commentator explains the rationale behind ordering contemplation from the closest to the most magnificent. This order is not arbitrary but rooted in Quranic precedent and pedagogical wisdom. As you read, notice how each step builds on the previous.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to the three stages mentioned.
2
Identify the three objects of contemplation
Note the order: self, upper world, lower world. The commentator explains the rationale for this order.
The order is not random.
3
Understand the purpose
Contemplation is a means to knowledge of God. The passage connects each stage to this ultimate goal.
The verse from Quran 2:164 supports the order.
4
Reflect on the methodology
Consider why starting with oneself is important and why the heavens precede the earth in the order.
Proximity and greatness are key factors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظرُ إلى العوالم وسيلة لمعرفة الحق سبحانه وتعالى]
١٥ - فَانْظُرْ إِلَى نَفْسِكَ ثُمَّ انْتَقِلِ لِلْعَالَمِ العُلْوِيِّ ثُمَّ السُّفْلِي قوله: (فَانْظُرْ ... إلخ)؛ أي: إذا أردت المعرفة فانظر ... إلى آخره؛ لأن النظر وسيلة لها ، والمأمور بالنظر كلُّ مكلَّف وأَمَرَهُ المصنِّفُ بالنظر إلى نفسه ابتداءً لأنها أقرب الأشياء)، ثم بالنظر إلى العالم العلوي لكونه أعظم وأبدع (٢) ، ثم إلى العالم السفلي، وفي تقديم العالم العلوي على السفلي اقتداء بقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ فِي خَلْقِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ . . [البقرة : ١٦٤) الآية .
The consideration of the worlds is a means to knowledge of the Truth (God) – may He be glorified and exalted. 15. So look at yourself, then proceed to the upper world, then the lower world. His saying: 'So look... etc.': i.e., if you want knowledge, look... to the end; because consideration is a means to it. And the one commanded to consider is every legally responsible person. The author commanded him to look at himself first because it is the nearest of things, then to look at the upper world because it is greater and more wondrous, then to the lower world. And in giving priority to the upper world over the lower, he follows the saying of the Exalted: 'Indeed in the creation of the heavens and the earth...' [Quran 2:164].
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the verse from the Jawharat al-Tawhid that commands looking at oneself, then the upper world, then the lower world. The commentator states that contemplation (النظر) of the worlds is a means to knowledge of God. He clarifies that every legally responsible person (مكلَّف) is commanded to perform this contemplation. The author of the poem (المصنف) ordered starting with oneself because it is the closest object of reflection. Then one proceeds to the upper world (the heavens) because it is greater and more wondrous in creation, and then to the lower world (the earth). The priority of the upper world over the lower follows the Quranic verse that mentions the creation of the heavens first. This order is not arbitrary but based on proximity and greatness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The order of contemplation is arbitrary or only the upper world matters.
Correction: The order is deliberate: start with oneself (nearest), then the heavens (greater), then the earth. All are important as means to knowledge of God.

Key Takeaway
The prescribed order of contemplation—starting with oneself, then the upper world, then the lower world—is a divinely guided methodology for attaining knowledge of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What one step will you take today to obey this command of reflection, not as an intellectual exercise, but as a life-changing encounter?

The Validity of Contemplation Does Not Require a Fixed Order
Commentator affirms flexibility in the order of contemplation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the validity of contemplation (nazar) does not depend on a specific order of the realms; any order is permissible.

Core Idea
Contemplation is valid regardless of order; flexibility is key.

The previous passage outlined a specific order for contemplating the self, the lower world, and the upper world. However, one might mistakenly think that order is a necessary condition for the act of contemplation to be valid. In this passage, the commentator clarifies that the validity of consideration does not depend on that order; any arrangement is acceptable as long as contemplation occurs. This prevents a rigid methodological misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the commentator's straightforward style.
2
Identify the core assertion
Find the phrase 'لا تتوقف صحة النظر على هذا الترتيب' and understand that it negates dependence on the order.
This is the main point of the passage.
3
Examine the examples
The passage gives two alternative sequences: self→lower→upper, and upper→lower→self. Note the 'other possible forms' clause shows infinite possibilities.
The examples illustrate the flexibility.
4
Reflect on the importance
Consider why this clarification is needed. It prevents a misunderstanding that the proof requires a rigid sequence.
Methodological rigidity can hinder proper reflection.
5
Summarize the takeaway
State in your own words: the validity of contemplation does not depend on order; any sequence is acceptable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تتوقف صحةُ النظر على هذا الترتيب بل يصح أن ينظر إلى النفس، ثم إلى العالم السفلي، ثم العلوي، أو ينظر إلى العالم العلوي، ثم إلى السفلي، ثم إلى النفس إلى غير ذلك من الصور الممكنة.

Commentary
This passage clarifies that the previously mentioned order (self, upper, lower) is not a necessary condition for the validity of contemplation. Any order of these three realms is acceptable. This flexibility prevents a misunderstanding that the proof requires a specific sequence. The commentator emphasises that what matters is the act of contemplation itself, not the arrangement.

The Stages of Knowledge according to al-Qushayri
al-Qushayri enumerates stages from correct speculation to action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sequential stages of spiritual and theological development as outlined by Imam al-Qushayri.

Core Idea
Correct speculation leads to certainty, then to heartfelt belief and sincere action.

This passage presents a profound teaching from Imam al-Qushayri that maps the journey from intellectual enquiry to embodied faith. Before we can act sincerely, we must first attain correct knowledge through sound reasoning, then reach certainty, then internalize belief, and finally express it through obedience. Let's read his words carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the sequence of stages mentioned by al-Qushayri.
The passage includes a later remark by Ibn Furak about the heart and tongue.
2
Identify the Stages
List the stages in order: correct speculation, certainty, belief (tasdiq), sincerity (ikhlas), showing testimony, and performing acts with tawhid.
Each stage builds on the previous one.
3
Reflect on the Heart-Tongue Metaphor
Consider Ibn Furak's statement that the tongue's utterance is an overflow from the heart. How does this connect to the stages?
The heart's fullness leads to verbal expression.
4
Apply the Concept
Think about your own spiritual journey. How can you move from mere speculation to certainty and sincere action?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتمثيل بها تمثيل للنظر الصحيح، فلا عبرة بالنظر الفاسد، قال الإمام القشيري في رسالته» ص ۳۱۸ : (أول الواجبات : هو المعرفة بالله سبحانه وتعالى والمعرفة لا تحصل إلا بتقديم شرائطها ؛ وهو النظر الصائب، ثم إذا توالت الأدلة وحصل البيان صار بتوالي الأنوار وحصول الاستبصار كالمُستغني عن تأمل البرهان وهو حال اليقين ثم تصديق الحق سبحانه فيما أخبر عند إصغائه إلى إجابة الداعي فيما يخبر من أفعاله سبحانه في المستأنف؛ لأن التصديق إنما يكون في الإخبار، ثم الإخلاص فيما يتعقبه من أداء الأوامر، ثم بعد ذلك إظهار الإجابة بجميل الشهادة ، ثم أداء الطاعات بالتوحيد فيما أمر به، والتجرد عمَّا زَجر عنه، وإلى هذا المعنى أشار الإمام أبو بكر بن فُورَك فيما سمعته يقول : ذكر اللسان فضلة يفيضُ عليها القلبُ) أي : يخرج منه على اللسان؛ لأن القلب متى امتلأ بشيء، نطق ببعضه اللسان، كذا ذكر شيخ الإسلام زكريا الأنصاري في إحكام الدلالة» (۷۷/۳).
And the example given is an example of correct reasoning, so no heed is paid to faulty reasoning. Imam al-Qushayri said in his 'Risala', p. 318: 'The first of obligations is knowledge of God Almighty, and knowledge is only attained by presenting its conditions, which is correct speculation. Then when the proofs follow and the explanation is obtained, by the succession of lights and attainment of insight he becomes as if independent of contemplation of the proof – this is the state of certainty. Then belief in the truth of God – exalted be He – regarding what He has informed, when he listens to the answer of the caller regarding what he informs of His actions in the future, because belief only occurs in reports. Then sincerity in what follows of performing commands. Then after that, showing the answer with beautiful testimony. Then performing acts of obedience with tawhid regarding what He commanded, and divesting from what He forbade. And to this meaning referred Imam Abu Bakr ibn Furak as I heard him say: 'The mention of the tongue is surplus that overflows from the heart' – i.e., it emerges from the heart onto the tongue, because when the heart is filled with something, the tongue speaks some of it. Thus mentioned Shaykh al-Islam Zakariyya al-Ansari in 'Ihkam al-Dalala' (3/77).'
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage contains a quote from Imam al-Qushayri describing the stages of spiritual development. First comes correct speculation (nazar sa'ib) as the condition for knowledge of God. Then, as proofs multiply and understanding becomes clear, one reaches a state of certainty (yaqin) where one no longer needs to actively ponder. After that comes believing in God's reports (tasdiq), then sincerity (ikhlas) in obeying commands, then showing the answer through beautiful testimony, and finally performing acts of obedience with tawhid and abandoning what is forbidden. The author then adds a remark from Ibn Furak that the tongue's utterance is an overflow from the heart, and Zakariyya al-Ansari confirms this.

Key Terms

يقين
تصديق
إخلاص
توحيد
قلب
لسان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that correct speculation alone is sufficient without reaching certainty and acting upon it.
Correction: Al-Qushayri shows that speculation is only the first step; it must lead to certainty, then to sincere action.

Checkpoint
According to al-Qushayri, what is the first of all obligations?
A) Prayer
B) Knowledge of God
C) Fasting
D) Zakat

Key Takeaway
True knowledge of God is not merely intellectual; it progresses through certainty, belief, sincerity, and culminates in obedient action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If someone listened to your conversations today, would they hear a heart filled with love for God and obedience to Him? What would need to change for your words to be an overflow of inner tawhid?

The Sufficiency of Mastering the Book
Author contrasts master of the book with blind follower.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the value of mastering this book for correct belief and the deficiency of blind imitation (taqlid).

Core Idea
Mastering a sound book suffices for creed; blind imitation is ignorance.

This brief passage draws a sharp contrast between two types of people: the one who holds fast to this book and masters it, and the blind follower who has not learned from scholars. The author emphasizes that true understanding, not mere acceptance, is the basis of correct belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the two parts: the description of the one who masters the book and the description of the blind follower.
Pay attention to the strong language used for the muqallid (like beasts).
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms التمسك (holding fast), الإتقان (mastery), المقلّد (blind follower), and العقائد (beliefs). Understand their meanings in this context.
These terms are foundational for the lesson.
3
Reflect on the contrast
Compare the two descriptions. What does the author want you to learn about seeking knowledge? The muqallid is criticized for not attending scholars and not distinguishing truth from falsehood.
Avoid common misunderstanding: the book is not a replacement for teachers, but mastery of it is sufficient.
4
Apply the lesson
Consider how this applies to your own learning. What does it mean to 'master' a book? It involves deep understanding, not just memorization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من تمسك به وأتقنه ، كفاه في عقائد إيمانه، وأما المقلّد فهو من لم يحضر العلماء، ولم يجلس بين أيديهم، ولا يعرف الأرض من السماء كالبهائم).
Whoever holds fast to it and masters it, it suffices him in the beliefs of his faith. As for the blind follower, he is one who has not attended the scholars, nor sat before them, and does not know earth from heaven, like beasts.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a brief exhortation about the value of the book. The author states that anyone who clings to this book and perfects it will find it sufficient for his creedal beliefs. In contrast, the blind follower (muqallid) who does not seek knowledge from scholars is likened to an animal that does not distinguish earth from sky. This emphasizes the importance of understanding and investigation (tahqiq) over mere imitation.

The Three Divisions of Kalam
The commentator enumerates the three branches of kalam and explains the logical order of their presentation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three classical divisions of kalam (Ilahiyyat, Nubuwwat, Sam'iyyat) and the logical reason for ordering them as they are.

Core Idea
Kalam is structured like a tree: existence of Allah is the root from which all other discussions branch.

Before diving into the detailed arguments of kalam, it is essential to map out its subject matter. The science is not a random collection of topics; it is divided into three clear branches based on what is being studied: matters relating to Allah, matters relating to prophets, and matters derived solely from revelation. Understanding this division helps you see how each topic fits into the bigger picture.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three divisions
Read the passage carefully and list the three divisions of kalam as enumerated by the commentator. Notice the Arabic terms: Ilahiyyat, Nubuwwat, Sam'iyyat.
These are the foundational categories of the entire science.
2
Understand the precedence of Ilahiyyat
The commentator explains why Ilahiyyat is placed first: because it deals directly with Allah, and what relates to Him takes priority over other topics.
3
Learn the priority within Ilahiyyat
Within Ilahiyyat, existence (al-wujud) is given precedence because it is the root; judgments about necessity, impossibility, and possibility depend on affirming existence first.
This mirrors the logical structure of kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد انقسمت مباحث هذا الفن ثلاثة أقسام: إلهيات: وهي المسائل المبحوث فيها عما يتعلق بالإله. ونبوات : وهي المسائل التي يُبحث فيها عما يتعلق بالأنبياء. وسمعيات وهي المسائل التي لا تُتلقَّى أحكامُها إلا من السمع. وقد شرع في تفصيل ذلك مقدماً الإلهيَّات على غيرها لتعلقها بالحقِّ تعالى، وما تعلق به مقدَّم على غيره، وبدأ بالواجب لشرفه، وإنما قدَّم منه الوجود لأنَّه كالأصل، وما عداه كالفرع ؛ لأنَّ الحكم بوجوب الواجبات له تعالى واستحالة المستحيلات عليه تعالى وجواز ما يجوز في حقه تعالى لا يُتعقَّلُ إلا بعد الحكم بوجوب الوجود له تعالى .
And the topics of this art have been divided into three divisions: Ilahiyyat: which are the subjects investigated concerning what relates to the Divine. And Nubuwwat: which are the subjects in which what relates to the prophets is investigated. And Sam'iyyat: which are the subjects whose rulings are only received from revelation. And he has embarked on detailing that, giving precedence to Ilahiyyat over others because of its connection to the Truth, the Exalted, and what is connected to Him is given precedence over other things, and he began with the necessary (attributes) due to its honor, and he gave precedence among them to existence because it is like the root, and what is other than it is like the branch; because the judgment of the obligation of the obligatorily true attributes for Him, the Exalted, and the impossibility of the impossible attributes for Him, the Exalted, and the possibility of what is possible in His right, the Exalted, cannot be conceived except after the judgment of the necessary existence for Him, the Exalted.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the topics of the science of kalam. The commentator states that the inquiries of this art are divided into three sections: Ilahiyyat (divine matters) concerning what relates to Allah; Nubuwwat (prophethood) concerning prophets; and Sam'iyyat (matters revealed) whose rulings are derived only from revelation. He then explains the author's order of presentation: Ilahiyyat is placed first due to its connection to Allah, and within Ilahiyyat, the necessary attributes are first, and within those, existence is first because it is the foundation; judgments of necessity, impossibility, and possibility for Allah are only conceivable after affirming His existence.

Key Terms
إلهيات
نبوات
سمعيات
الواجب
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the divisions of kalam are arbitrary or simply traditional.
Correction: The divisions are based on subject matter: what relates to Allah, prophets, and revelation. The order is logical, reflecting priority and dependence.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is existence given precedence among the necessary attributes?
A) Because it is the most honorable attribute.
B) Because it is like the root, and other attributes are like branches.
C) Because it is the first attribute mentioned in the poem.
D) Because it is the only attribute that is necessary.

Key Takeaway
The three divisions of kalam are Ilahiyyat, Nubuwwat, and Sam'iyyat, with Ilahiyyat prioritized because it deals directly with Allah, and within Ilahiyyat, existence is the foundation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly treat the belief in Allah's existence as the root of your entire worldview, or do you sometimes let secondary theological discussions overshadow this foundation? What can you do today to strengthen this root?
?
Knowing that prophethood and revelation depend on first knowing Allah, how does this reshape the priority of your learning? Are you investing enough time in understanding Allah's attributes before delving into other topics?
?
The passage argues that you cannot even conceive of Allah's other attributes without first affirming His existence. In your daily life, are your actions and decisions rooted in the reality of Allah's existence, or do you act as if He is distant? Commit to one specific action today that makes His existence central.

The Three Divisions of Kalam and Their Order
The author divides the topics of kalam into Ilahiyyat, Nubuwwat, and Sam'iyyat and explains the rationale for their order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main divisions of kalam (Ilahiyyat, Nubuwwat, Sam'iyyat) and the logical order in which they are studied.

Core Idea
The topics of kalam are organized by their subject: God, prophets, and revelation.

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of the science of kalam. The author divides all kalam topics into three categories based on their subject matter: matters concerning God (Ilahiyyat), matters concerning prophets (Nubuwwat), and matters known only through revelation (Sam'iyyat). He then explains why Ilahiyyat comes first and within it, why existence is prioritized.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three divisions
Read the Arabic passage and locate the three categories: Ilahiyyat, Nubuwwat, Sam'iyyat. Note how each is defined.
The definitions are given concisely.
2
Understand the precedence of Ilahiyyat
The author states that Ilahiyyat are given precedence because they relate to Allah. Reflect on why this is logical.
3
Understand precedence within Ilahiyyat
Within Ilahiyyat, the Necessary Being is discussed first, and within that, existence is first because it is the foundation. Why is existence the foundation?
This is because judgments of necessity, impossibility, and contingency depend on establishing existence.
4
Examine the grammatical note
The author comments on the phrase 'wajib al-wujud', noting that 'wajib' is the subject and 'al-wujud' is the predicate, giving emphasis to existence.
This grammatical point reinforces the priority of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد انقسمت مباحث هذا الفن ثلاثة أقسام: إلهيات: وهي المسائل المبحوث فيها عما يتعلق بالإله. ونبوات: وهي المسائل التي يُبحث فيها عما يتعلق بالأنبياء. وسمعيات: وهي المسائل التي لا تُتلقَّى أحكامُها إلا من السمع. وقد شرع في تفصيل ذلك مقدماً الإلهيَّات على غيرها لتعلقها بالحقِّ تعالى، وما تعلق به مقدَّم على غيره، وبدأ بالواجب لشرفه، وإنما قدَّم منه الوجود لأنَّه كالأصل، وما عداه كالفرع؛ لأنَّ الحكم بوجوب الواجبات له تعالى واستحالة المستحيلات عليه تعالى وجواز ما يجوز في حقه تعالى لا يُتعقَّلُ إلا بعد الحكم بوجوب الوجود له تعالى. ثم إن المصنف قدَّمَ الخبر للاهتمام؛ لأنَّ المقصود الحكم بالوجوب، وقد يُقال: الظاهرُ إعراب (واجب) مبتدأ، وسَوَّغَ الابتداء به مع كونه نكرة عمله في الجار والمجرور، و(الوجود) وما بعده خبر، فكأنه قال: الواجب المتقدم ذكره هو الوجود وما عطف عليه.
And the topics of this art have divided into three sections: Ilahiyyat (divine matters): which are the topics investigated concerning what pertains to the Divine. And Nubuwwat (prophethood matters): which are the topics investigated concerning what pertains to the prophets. And Sam'iyyat (audible matters): which are the topics whose rulings are only received from audition (revelation). And he began the detailing of that, giving precedence to the Ilahiyyat over others because they relate to the Truth, the Exalted, and what relates to Him is given precedence over others. And he began with the Necessary (al-wajib) for its honor, and he gave precedence from it to existence because it is like the origin, and what is besides it is like the branch; because the judgment of the necessity of necessary things for Him, the Exalted, and the impossibility of impossible things for Him, the Exalted, and the contingency of what is possible in His right, the Exalted, cannot be conceived except after the judgment of the necessity of existence for Him, the Exalted. Then the author gave precedence to the predicate for attention because the intended is the judgment of necessity. And it may be said: the apparent grammatical analysis is that (wajib) is a subject, and its initial position despite being indefinite is allowed by its operation in the prepositional phrase, and (al-wujud) and what follows it is the predicate, as if he said: the previously mentioned necessary is existence and what is conjoined to it.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the topics of the science of kalam. The author divides them into three categories: Ilahiyyat (matters concerning the Divine), Nubuwwat (matters concerning prophets), and Sam'iyyat (matters known only through revelation). He then explains the rationale for ordering the discussion: Ilahiyyat are given precedence because they relate directly to God, and within Ilahiyyat, he begins with the Necessary Being (al-wajib) because of its honor. Further, within the treatment of the Necessary, existence (al-wujud) is prioritized because it is the foundation—the judgments of necessity, impossibility, and contingency for God can only be understood after establishing the necessity of His existence. The author also notes a grammatical point: in the phrase 'wajib al-wujud', the word 'wajib' is a subject and 'al-wujud' is a predicate, and this construction gives emphasis to the predicate (al-wujud) for the sake of attention, as the intended meaning is the judgment of necessity of existence.

Key Terms
إلهيات
نبوات
سمعيات
واجب الوجود
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the three divisions are arbitrary or based on convenience.
Correction: The divisions are based on the source of knowledge: reason for Ilahiyyat and Nubuwwat, and revelation for Sam'iyyat, and the order is logically justified.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are Ilahiyyat given precedence over Nubuwwat and Sam'iyyat?
A) Because they are more numerous.
B) Because they relate directly to God, the Truth.
C) Because they are easier to understand.
D) Because they are mentioned first in the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
The study of kalam begins with the existence of God because it is the foundation for all other rational judgments concerning Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own belief in Allah, do you prioritize affirming His existence as the foundation, or do you find yourself focusing on other aspects first? Why does this order matter for the strength of your faith?
?
The author argues that judgments about what is necessary, impossible, and possible for Allah depend on first establishing His existence. Have you ever reflected on whether your understanding of Allah's attributes is built on a clear conviction of His existence? How secure is that foundation in your heart?
?
If you were to teach someone about Allah, would you begin with the proof of His existence? What would you lose if you skipped that step? Commit to one action today to strengthen your certainty in Allah's existence.

The Critical Status of the Proofs of Infinite Regress and Circularity
Warns against denying the validity of the proofs of batalan al-tasalsul and al-dawr, citing refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proofs of batalan al-tasalsul (infinite regress) and al-dawr (circularity) are considered foundational to kalam, and the danger of denying their validity.

Core Idea
Denying the proof of infinite regress undermines the entire edifice of kalam arguments.

This passage is a critical note from the commentator addressing a controversial opinion among some modern scholars, including Muhammad Abduh and Muhammad Bakhit al-Muti'i, who disparaged the proof of infinite regress. The author stresses that mastering these two proofs is of utmost importance because the majority of kalam proofs rely on them. He then critiques al-Muti'i's related erroneous views while acknowledging his scholarly standing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the literal translation. Notice the author's tone of warning.
2
Identify the core claim
Find the statement about the importance of mastering the two proofs. The author says most kalam proofs depend on them.
3
Analyze the critique
Examine the criticism of al-Muti'i's views. What are the specific errors mentioned? Why does the author call them 'displeasing opinions'?
The author maintains respect despite disagreement, ending with a prayer for forgiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : ذهب بعض المحدثين كالشيخ محمد عبدو رحمه الله تعالى إلى استبعاد أو إنكار وجود دليل على بطلان التسلسل، وقد نبه العلماء إلى أن إتقان دليلي بطلان التسلسل والدور من أهم الأمور ; إذ على ذلك تعويل أكثر أدلة المتكلمين وقد مالَ إلى قول الشيخ محمد عبدو عصريه العلامة التحرير مفتي الديار المصرية الشيخ محمد بخيت المطيعي، حيث قال كلمة غير مرضية : (إن بطلان التسلسل لغاية اليوم لم يقم عليه دليل برهاني سالم مما يبطله !!) ، وله مع هذه الكلمة آراء منغصة ; كقوله بتعليل أفعاله تعالى بالأغراض والمصالح، ومسألة افتقار الممكن بعضه إلى بعض، وكفى المرء نبلاً أن تعد معايبه، وإنما كان التنبيه لأن العلامة المطيعي علم كبير له منة في أعناق من جاء بعده، وهذه الهنات تقصى غفر الله لنا جميعاً .
A note: Some modernists such as Sheikh Muhammad Abduh (may Allah have mercy on him) went to the extent of deeming improbable or denying the existence of a proof for the invalidity of infinite regress. Scholars have pointed out that mastering the two proofs of the invalidity of infinite regress and circularity is among the most important matters; because most of the proofs of the mutakallimun depend on that. And his contemporary, the erudite scholar, the Grand Mufti of Egypt, Sheikh Muhammad Bakhit al-Muti'i, inclined to the opinion of Sheikh Muhammad Abduh, he said an unsatisfactory statement: 'The invalidity of infinite regress until today has not been established by a demonstrative proof free from what nullifies it!!' And along with this statement he has some displeasing opinions, such as his saying that Allah's actions are caused by purposes and benefits, and the issue of the contingency dependence upon one another. And sufficient for a man's nobility is that his faults are counted. The warning is because al-Muti'i is a great scholar who has a favor on the necks of those who came after him, but these flaws are rejected. May Allah forgive us all.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a critical note (تنبيه) appended to the commentary on the proof of batalan al-tasalsul. It addresses a dangerous trend among some modernists, particularly Sheikh Muhammad Abduh and his follower Sheikh Muhammad Bakhit al-Muti'i, who denied that there is a sound proof for the impossibility of infinite regress. The author stresses that mastering the proofs of batalan al-tasalsul and al-dawr (circularity) is extremely important because most arguments of the kalam scholars (mutakallimun) rely on them. The author then criticizes al-Muti'i for this and other erroneous views, such as attributing purposes and benefits to Allah's actions, and the idea of contingency dependence upon one another. Despite al-Muti'i's scholarly status, the author rejects these opinions. The tone is respectful but firm, and ends with a prayer for forgiveness.

Key Terms
بطلان التسلسل
الدور
المتكلمون
المحدثون
محمد عبده
محمد بخيت المطيعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of infinite regress is not important and can be dismissed without harming kalam.
Correction: The author shows that this proof is foundational; most kalam arguments depend on it, so denying it undermines the entire theological method.

Checkpoint

A) Because they are the only proofs for God's existence.

D) Because they are easy to understand.

Key Takeaway
The proofs of infinite regress and circularity are foundational to kalam; denying them jeopardizes the entire theological project.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Minimal Knowledge of God's Existence
States the minimal theological obligation for a mukallaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the minimum required belief for a mukallaf is simply to know that God exists, without needing to unravel deeper metaphysical questions.

Core Idea
Faith begins with knowing God exists, not with solving theological complexities.

This passage clarifies a crucial principle in Islamic theology: not every believer is required to delve into the finer points of kalam. Your obligation is simpler than you might think. Let's see what Sanusi's commentator says about the minimal knowledge of God's existence for the mukallaf.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the English translation. Let the author's own words set the context.
2
Identify the two key points
What is required (knowledge of existence) and what is not obligatory (subtle questions about essence). Notice the clear contrast.
The passage uses 'يكفي... ولا يجب...' structure.
3
Reflect on the example given
Consider the question: is God's existence identical to His essence or not? This is given as an example of an obscure matter not required for every believer.
This question is a real debate in kalam, but the point is its non-obligatory status.
4
Understand the term 'ghawamid'
The passage calls such questions 'obscure matters' (غوامض). Reflect on why these are considered beyond the basic duty.
Ghawamid are subtleties that require specialized study.
5
Connect to your own learning
How does this principle shape your approach to studying kalam? It sets a boundary between essential belief and advanced inquiry.
Remember: the goal is not to make faith complicated, but to safeguard it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويكفي المكلَّف أن يعرف أن الله موجودٌ، ولا يجب عليه معرفة أن وجودَهُ تعالى عين ذاته أو غيرُ ذاته كما قال سيدي محمد الصُّغَيَّر؛ لأنَّ ذلك من غوامض علم الكلام.
And it suffices for the legally responsible person to know that God exists, and it is not obligatory for him to know whether His existence, exalted is He, is identical to His essence or other than His essence, as said by my master Muhammad al-Sughayyar; because that is among the obscure matters of the science of kalam.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage defines the minimal theological obligation for a mukallaf (legally responsible person). It states that only knowledge of God's existence is required, not the finer speculative details. The example given is the question whether God's existence is identical to His essence (i.e., no real distinction) or other than His essence (i.e., a separate attribute). This question is considered one of the 'obscure matters' (ghawamid) of kalam, meaning it is not obligatory for every believer to delve into. It cites the authority of Muhammad al-Sughayyar. The passage thus emphasizes the difference between essential belief and advanced theological investigation.

B) To know that God's existence is other than His essence

D) To understand all obscure matters of kalam

Key Takeaway
The minimal requirement of faith is simply to know that God exists; investigating deeper questions is not obligatory for every believer.

Why Kalam Scholars Explicitly State Implied Attributes
Objection and answer on explicit mention of implied attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kalam scholars explicitly state attributes like qadim and baqa even when they are implied by wajib al-wujud, and the methodological principle behind this practice.

Core Idea
Explicit statement prevents ignorance in creed; implication is not enough.

An objector argues that mentioning qadim and baqa after wajub al-wujud is redundant because they are implied. The answer reveals a key methodological principle: scholars of kalam do not rely on implication alone. Because the stakes are high—ignorance in creed is dangerous—they state each belief explicitly. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector claims that since wajub al-wujud implies qadim and baqa, mentioning them separately is redundant. Why might someone think that?
2
Identify the answer
The scholars do not rely on implication (dalalah al-iltizam). They explicitly state beliefs due to the danger of ignorance.
3
Grasp the principles
No substitution of malzum for lazim, nor general for specific. This is a key pedagogical rule.
4
Reflect on the methodology
Consider how this approach safeguards creed and ensures clarity in teaching.
This shows the careful pedagogical approach of kalam texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : إن وجوب الوجود يستلزم القدم، بل والبقاء فذكرهما بعده محض تكرار؟ قلت : علماء هذا الفنّ لا يكتفون بدلالة الالتزام، بل يصرحون بالعقائدِ لشدَّة خطر الجهل في هذا الفن، فلا يستغنون بملزوم عن لازم، ولا بعام عن خاص.
If you say: 'Necessity of existence implies qadim, indeed also baqa (endlessness), so mentioning them after it is mere repetition?' I say: The scholars of this art are not content with the indication of implication; rather, they explicitly state beliefs due to the extreme danger of ignorance in this art. So they do not dispense with a necessary concomitant (lazim) in place of its necessitator (malzum), nor with a general in place of a specific.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues: Since Necessity of Existence (wajib al-wujud) already implies qadim (eternity) and baqa (endlessness), mentioning those attributes separately after it is mere repetition. The answer replies that scholars of kalam do not rely on implicit implication (dalalah al-iltizam). Because the danger of ignorance in matters of creed is severe, they explicitly state each belief. They do not substitute a necessary concomitant (lazim) for its necessitator (malzum), nor a general for a specific. In other words, they list every attribute directly rather than expecting the reader to derive it from another.

Key Terms
دلالة الالتزام
ملزوم
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is sufficient to derive attributes by implication from a more general attribute.
Correction: Scholars insist on explicit mention because the danger of ignorance in creed is severe, and they do not rely on implication alone.

Checkpoint
Why do kalam scholars explicitly mention qadim and baqa after wajub al-wujud, even though they are implied?
A) Because implication is logically invalid
B) Because of the extreme danger of ignorance in creed
C) Because they do not understand implication
D) Because wajub al-wujud does not actually imply them

Key Takeaway
Kalam scholars explicitly state each attribute because the danger of ignorance in creed is severe; they do not rely on implied meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted that Allah is Eternal and Endless simply because you knew He is Necessarily Existent, without consciously affirming these attributes in your heart? After this lesson, will you now take the time to explicitly affirm each of His attributes, knowing the danger of ignorance?
?
When you learn about a new attribute of Allah, do you ever rely on a vague implication rather than seeking a clear understanding? This lesson shows that scholars do not take that risk—where is the line between trust in Allah's mercy and negligence in seeking knowledge in your own life?
?
If the danger of ignorance in creed is as severe as these scholars claim, what one attribute of Allah will you intentionally learn and teach to someone else this week, so that neither you nor they rely on mere implication?

Answer to the Objection of Redundancy
Reply to the objection that mentioning qidam and baqa' after wujub is redundant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kalam scholars explicitly state each attribute rather than relying on implication, and why mentioning qidam and baqa' after wujub is not redundant.

Core Idea
In kalam, explicit statement guards against ignorance; implication is not enough.

This passage presents a common objection: if necessity of existence implies pre-eternity and permanence, then listing them separately is redundant. The author's reply reveals a key methodological principle in kalam: scholars do not rely on implied meanings because the stakes are too high. Instead, they state every belief explicitly to eliminate any chance of misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part where the objector claims redundancy. Notice the logic: wajib implies qidam and baqa, so mentioning them after is repetition.
2
Understand the reply
Read the author's answer. Note the key phrase 'لا يكتفون بدلالة الالتزام' – they are not content with implication.
This is the core methodological point.
3
Identify the principle
The author gives two examples: they do not replace a necessary consequence (lazim) with its entailer (malzum), nor a general with a specific. This shows they prioritize explicit clarity.
Think of examples from other sciences where implication might be acceptable but not in kalam.
4
Reflect on the danger
Why is the danger of ignorance so severe in this art? Because beliefs about Allah must be precise; any ambiguity could lead to misbelief.
This explains the methodological choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : إن وجوب الوجود يستلزم القدم، بل والبقاء فذكرهما بعده محض تكرار؟ قلت : علماء هذا الفنّ لا يكتفون بدلالة الالتزام، بل يصرحون بالعقائدِ لشدَّة خطر الجهل في هذا الفن، فلا يستغنون بملزوم عن لازم، ولا بعام عن خاص.
If you say: The necessity of existence implies pre-eternity, indeed also permanence, so mentioning them after it is pure repetition? I say: The scholars of this art are not content with implication by entailment, rather they explicitly state beliefs due to the severity of the danger of ignorance in this art, so they do not dispense with a necessary consequence in place of the thing which entails it, nor with a general in place of a specific.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage records an objection and its answer. The objector claims that since wajib al-wujud (the Necessary Existent) necessarily implies qidam (pre-eternity) and baqa' (permanence), repeating these attributes after mentioning wujub is redundant. The author replies that kalam scholars do not rely on implied meanings (dalalat al-iltizam) because the danger of ignorance in this field is grave. Instead, they explicitly state each belief. They do not replace a necessary consequence (lazim) with its entailer (malzum), nor a specific (khas) with a general (amm). Thus, mentioning qidam and baqa' explicitly is not redundant but necessary for clarity.

Correction: The scholars of kalam explicitly state each belief because the danger of ignorance is severe; they do not rely on implication alone.

Checkpoint

C) Because the Quran commands explicit declaration.

Key Takeaway
In kalam, explicit formulation is necessary to eliminate any possibility of error due to the high stakes of theological knowledge.
