The Qur'anic Verse as a Decisive Proof: A Debate with al-Sa'd
Debate on the nature of a Qur'anic proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Qur'anic verse (21:22) is considered a decisive rational proof against the existence of multiple gods, and how al-Sa'd's alternative interpretation was refuted.

Core Idea
The verse is rationally decisive because two absolute dominators cannot coexist.

In this lesson, we explore a key debate in Islamic theology: whether the Qur'anic verse 'If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would have been corrupted' is a decisive rational proof or merely a persuasive argument. The author affirms the former, refuting al-Sa'd al-Taftāzānī's view, and reinforces the proof with a powerful argument from divine nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Pay attention to the footnotes and the quoted verse.
The passage contains a debate, so note the different voices.
2
Identify the two positions
The author states that the verse is a decisive proof (ḥujja qaṭʿiyya), while al-Saʿd held it is rhetorical (iqnāʿiyya).
Mark where each position is stated.
3
Understand al-Sa'd's reasoning
Al-Sa'd interpreted 'fasād' as deviation from order, not non-existence, making the implication contingent on actual conflict.
This interpretation makes the argument merely persuasive.
4
Examine criticism and defense
Learn about the strong criticism, including a charge of disbelief, and the defense by al-Sa'd's student ʿAlāʾ al-Dīn.
The defense notes that the Quran contains both decisive and rhetorical proofs.
5
Grasp the final reinforcement
The author concludes that even if initial agreement between gods is conceivable, reflection shows it's impossible because divinity requires absolute dominance.
The verse from Surah al-Muʾminūn supports this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينبني على ذلك أن الآيةَ حجّة قطعية (۲)، وهو التحقيق، خلافاً لما جرى عليه السعد من أنها إقناعية (۳) ؛ أي: يقنع بها الخصم، مع كون التلازم فيها ليس عقليًّا؛ بناءً على تفسير الفسادِ فيها بالخروج عن النظام، وإنَّما لم يكن التلازم فيها عقليّا على هذا لأنه لا يلزمُ حصول الفساد بالفعل، وقد شنّعوا على السعد في ذلك، حتى قال عبد اللطيف الكرماني : إنه تعييب لبراهين القرآن، وهو كفر !). وأجابَ علاء الدين تلميذُ السعد بأن القرآن يحتوي على الأدلة الإقناعية لمطابقة حال بعض القاصرين. وتجويز الاتفاق إنَّما هو ببادئ الرأي، وعند التأمل لا يصح صلح بين إلهين ؛ إذ مرتبةُ الألوهية تقتضي الغلبة المطلقة، كما يشير قوله تعالى: ﴿لَذَهَبَ كُلَّ إِلَم بِمَا خَلَقَ وَلَعَلَا بَعْضُهُمْ عَلَى بَعض) [المؤمنون: ١٩١].
And upon that it follows that the verse is a decisive proof (2) - and that is the verified position - contrary to what al-Sa'd held that it is a rhetorical proof (3), i.e., it convinces the opponent, while the implication (talāzum) in it is not rational; based on his interpretation of 'corruption' (fasād) in it as deviation from order. And it is not rational on this view because corruption does not necessarily occur in actuality. And they strongly criticized al-Sa'd for that, such that ʿAbd al-Laṭīf al-Kirmānī said: 'It is disparagement of the proofs of the Qur'an, and it is unbelief!' And ʿAlāʾ al-Dīn, al-Sa'd's student, replied that the Qur'an contains rhetorical proofs to suit the state of some deficient minds. And the allowance of agreement [between two gods] is only in the first impression; upon reflection, no reconciliation between two gods is possible, because the station of divinity necessitates absolute dominance, as indicated by His statement: 'Then each god would have taken what he created, and some of them would have risen above others' (al-Muʾminūn 91).
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage addresses the debate over whether the Qur'anic verse (21:22) is a decisive rational proof (ḥujja qaṭʿiyya) or merely a rhetorical argument (iqnāʿiyya). The author affirms the former, noting that al-Saʿd al-Taftāzānī held the latter view because he interpreted 'fasād' as 'deviation from order' rather than non-existence, making the implication contingent on actual conflict. The author reports severe criticism of al-Saʿd, including a charge of disbelief by ʿAbd al-Laṭīf al-Kirmānī. Al-Saʿd's student, ʿAlāʾ al-Dīn, defended his teacher by noting that the Qur'an contains rhetorical proofs for weaker intellects. The author then reinforces the proof by rejecting the possibility of two gods agreeing: even if initially conceivable, thorough reflection shows that two absolute dominators cannot coexist—each would necessarily overpower the other, as another verse indicates.

Key Terms
حجة قطعية
إقناعية
فساد
التلازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse is only a persuasive argument and not a rational proof.
Correction: The author demonstrates that, properly understood, the verse provides a decisive rational proof because the implication is necessary when 'fasād' means non-existence and because the nature of divinity precludes coexistence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did al-Sa'd consider the verse to be iqna'iyyah (rhetorical)?
A) Because the verse is linguistically ambiguous
B) Because he interpreted 'fasād' as departure from order, not non-existence
C) Because he thought the verse contradicts rationality
D) Because he believed all Qur'anic proofs are rhetorical

Key Takeaway
The verse is rationally decisive because the impossibility of two gods is grounded in the nature of divinity itself, not merely in contingent observable corruption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite verse 21:22, do you feel its logical certainty in your heart, or do you still entertain the possibility that another deity might exist? What would it take for you to truly internalize that no one else shares even an atom of Allah's absolute dominance?
?
Al-Sa'd considered the verse merely persuasive to accommodate weaker minds. Have you ever reduced a clear Qur'anic proof to something less decisive to fit your own intellectual comfort? How can you guard against softening Allah's arguments?
?
The debate hinged on what 'fasād' means. When you read 'both would have been corrupted', do you immediately think of physical disorder, or do you grasp the deeper logical corruption of divinity itself? Which understanding moves you to more profound tawḥīd?

Grammatical Analysis of 'Munazzahan' and 'Saniyya'
The commentator explains the grammatical function and meaning of two words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical function (circumstantial accusative) and the theological meaning of two key words in the poem.

Core Idea
Arabic grammar reveals deeper theological meaning in kalam texts.

The commentator unpacks two words from the poem, showing how grammatical structures reinforce theological points. This lesson explores that analysis, focusing on the roles of 'munazzahan' and 'saniyya' as circumstantial accusatives and the nuanced meanings of 'saniyya'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overview of the commentator's explanation.
Pay attention to the grammatical terms used.
2
Identify the grammatical roles
Note that 'munazzahan' is identified as a circumstantial accusative (ḥāl) from the pronoun in 'fawājib'. Similarly, 'awṣāfuhu saniyya' is another circumstantial accusative, either synonymous or overlapping.
Think about how this grammatical structure emphasizes God's transcendence.
3
Understand the meaning of 'saniyya'
The commentator gives two possible meanings: (1) resembling light ('sanā') metaphorically, or (2) meaning lofty ('saniyya' from 'sanā' with long vowel). Understand why the literal light interpretation is rejected.
The key point is that attributes cannot literally be light, as light is an accident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (منزَّهاً) حال من الضمير في قوله : ( فواجب) له ... إلى آخره)، فالمعنى: أنه تعالى وجبَتْ له هذه الصفاتُ حال كونه منزّهاً، فهي حال لازمةٌ ؛ مثل: دعوت الله سميعاً، وهي مؤكّدةً للصفات السابقة، وكذلك جملة قوله: (أوصافه سنيه) فهي حال أيضاً من الضمير المذكور، فهي حال مترادفة، ويجوز أن تكون حالاً من الضمير في (منزّهاً) فهي حال متداخلةٌ . (ومعنى قوله : (سنيَّه) أنها تشبه السَّنا - بالقصر - وهو النور، بجامع الاهتداء، فيُهتدى بها ؛ أي: بأثرها ؛ لأنه المشاهد لنا، كما يهتدى بالسَّنا الذي هو النور، فالنسبة على وجه التشبيه وليس المراد أنه قام بها السنا وهو النورُ ؛ لأن النور عرض يستحيل قيامه بالصفة، أو معناه : رفيعةٌ، فيكون لفظ (سنيه) مأخوذاً من السناء - بالمد يمعنى الرفعة، والمراد الرفعة المعنوية.
His statement: 'munazzahan' is a circumstantial accusative from the pronoun in his statement: 'fawājib' ... etc. So the meaning: He, exalted, has these attributes necessary for Him while being exalted, so it is a permanent circumstantial accusative; like: 'I called God, hearing.' And it is emphatic for the preceding attributes. Similarly, the clause 'awṣāfuhu saniyya' is also a circumstantial accusative from the mentioned pronoun, so it is a synonymous circumstantial accusative, and it is permissible for it to be a circumstantial accusative from the pronoun in 'munazzahan', so it is an overlapping circumstantial accusative. And the meaning of his statement: 'saniyya' is that they resemble 'sanā' (with short alif), which is light, due to the commonality of guidance; so they are guided by them, i.e., by their effect, because it is what we perceive, just as one is guided by 'sanā' which is light. So the relation is by way of simile, and it is not intended that 'sanā' which is light has occurred in them, because light is an accident which is impossible to subsist in an attribute. Or its meaning is 'lofty', so the word 'saniyya' is taken from 'sanā' (with long alif) meaning loftiness, and what is intended is conceptual loftiness.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and semantic analysis. The commentator identifies 'munazzahan' as a circumstantial accusative (ḥāl) indicating that God's necessary attributes are affirmed while He is transcendent. The second phrase 'awṣāfuhu saniyya' is also a circumstantial accusative, either synonymous or overlapping. Then the meaning of 'saniyya' is explored: it can be likened to 'sanā' (light) metaphorically, guiding people, but literal light is an accident that cannot inhere in an attribute. The preferred interpretation is that 'saniyya' means lofty, derived from 'sanā' with long vowel, indicating the high status of God's attributes.

Key Terms
حال
منزهاً
سنيه
السنا
النور
السناء
الرفعة
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'saniyya' literally means that God's attributes are light.
Correction: The commentator explains it is metaphorical (tashbīh) and not literal because light is an accident that cannot subsist in an attribute.

Checkpoint
What grammatical function does 'munazzahan' serve in the poem?
A) Adjective
B) Circumstantial accusative
C) Object
D) Subject

Key Takeaway
In this passage, 'munazzahan' and 'saniyya' are circumstantial accusatives that emphasize God's transcendence and the loftiness of His attributes, with 'saniyya' understood metaphorically or as derived from loftiness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Where in your life this week have you actually allowed God's attributes—like His mercy or justice—to shape your actions? What holds you back from letting them guide you more directly?
?
The commentator says that God's attributes guide us through their effects, not directly. The Analogy Between Shar'i and Rational Proofs
Explains the analogy between shar'i and rational evidence for attribute distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinction of divine attributes is established by both revealed and rational proofs, and why the analogy between them is sound.

Core Idea
Shar'i and rational proofs confirm the same truth without contradiction.

In this short passage, the commentator clarifies the phrase 'as it is established' by showing that the distinction of attributes (taghayyur) is not unique to one proof. He draws an analogy between the distinction derived from Shar'i evidence and that from rational evidence, and addresses a potential objection about identifying the two. The verse uses a simile (tashbih) to affirm that both sources confirm the same reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and the issue
Read the Arabic passage and notice the phrase 'kama thabat'. The commentator is explaining what this refers to. Understand that there is a potential objection: the simile might imply identity of the two things compared.
The objection is about 'tashbih' leading to 'itihad' (identification).
2
First interpretation
The commentator offers that the distinction from Shar'i proof is the simile, and the distinction from rational proof is the thing compared. This keeps them distinct while affirming both.
This interpretation treats the distinction as the point of comparison.
3
Second interpretation
Alternatively, the simile is the distinction mentioned in the author's own speech (the poem), and the thing compared is the distinction established with Ahl al-Sunna. This avoids the objection by relating it to the text itself.
Both interpretations preserve the non-identity of the two terms.
4
Consider the causal reading
The kaf may be interpreted causally, changing the meaning to 'because of the proof that is rationally established'. This reading emphasizes the rational proof as the basis.
This shows the flexibility of the Arabic particle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (كَمَا ثَبَتْ) أي: كالتغاير الذي ثبت، لا يُقال : فيه اتحاد المشبه والمشبه به؛ لأنا نقول : المعنى وغايرت ما ذكر شرعاً كما ثبت عقلاً، فالتغايرُ المستفاد من الدليل الشرعي مشبه والتغاير الثابت بالدليل العقلي مشبه به، أو يقال : المشبه هو التغاير المذكور في كلام المصنف والمشبه به هو التغاير الثابت عند أهل السنة، ويصح أن تكون الكاف للتعليل، و(ما) واقعة على الدليل، فيكون المعنى : للدليل الذي ثبت عقلاً.
His saying: 'as it is established' means: like the distinction that is established. It cannot be said: there is identification of the simile and the thing compared. For we say: the meaning is that it is distinct from what was mentioned according to shar' (revelation) as it is established according to reason; so the distinction derived from the shar'i proof is the simile, and the distinction established by the rational proof is the thing compared. Or it is said: the simile is the distinction mentioned in the author's speech, and the thing compared is the distinction established with Ahl al-Sunna. And it is possible that the kaf is for explanation, and 'ma' refers to the proof, so the meaning is: for the proof that was established rationally.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'as it is established' (kama thabat) from the poem. It clarifies that the comparison (tashbih) here is not problematic; it is an analogy between the distinction of attributes proven by revelation (shar') and that proven by reason ('aql). The author offers two interpretations: (1) the shari distinction is the simile, the rational distinction is the thing compared; (2) the distinction mentioned in the text (the poet's words) is compared to the Sunni creedal distinction. Alternatively, the kaf may be causal, meaning 'because of the proof that is rationally established.'

Key Terms
تغاير (taghayyur)
دليل شرعي (dalil shar'i)
دليل عقلي (dalil 'aqli)
تشبيه (tashbih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the analogy implies a defect in the proof or that one proof is inferior.
Correction: The analogy simply shows both sources confirm the same truth; it does not weaken either proof.

Checkpoint
According to one interpretation in the commentary, what does the phrase 'as it is established' refer to?
A) The distinction from rational proof only
B) The distinction from shari proof is the simile
C) The kaf is for emphasis
D) The author is comparing two different doctrines

Key Takeaway
The distinction of divine attributes is confirmed by both revelation and reason, and the analogy between them is a valid rhetorical device.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Evidence for Divine Speech is Transmitted
Al-Ajhuri explains the distinction in evidence for God's Speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the evidence for God's attribute of Speech is primarily transmitted (naqli) rather than rational ('aqli).

Core Idea
The attribute of Speech is proven by revelation, not reason alone.

In previous lessons, we learned that most of God's attributes are established through rational evidence, sometimes supported by revelation. But the attribute of Speech is different. Al-Ajhuri now explains that its primary proof comes from transmitted texts, although reason can play a supporting role. Let's examine his explanation closely.

Guided Path
1
Parse the syntactical structure
Notice the inverted syntax: 'كَذَا الكَلامُ' where 'كَذَا' is a forward predicate and 'الكَلامُ' is a delayed subject. This emphasizes that Speech is 'like that'—referring back to the previous attributes.
Arabic word order often conveys emphasis.
2
Understand the comparison
The phrase 'الكلام مثل ذا' means Speech is similar to the previous attributes in absolute necessity for God, but not in every respect. The key difference is in the type of evidence used.
All attributes are necessary, but the method of proof may differ.
3
Distinguish the evidence types
The previous attributes primarily rely on rational evidence, either alone or with transmitted support for emphasis. In contrast, the evidence for Speech is primarily transmitted, with rational evidence only for emphasis. The main reliance is on 'al-dalīl al-samʿī' (heard evidence).
Transmitted evidence means scripture and revelation.
4
Connect to the upcoming line
Al-Ajhuri points to the author's later line 'بذي أتانا السمع' as the proof text, reinforcing that the source for Speech is what revelation brings us.
This anticipates the scriptural evidence for God's Speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (كَذَا الكَلامُ ) ( كذا) خبرٌ ،مقدَّم ، و (الكلام) مبتدأ مؤخر، والمعنى: الكلام مثل ذا ؛ أي مثل ما تقدم من الصفات والتشبيه ليس من كل وجه بل في مطلق الوجوب الله تعالى وإن خالفها في الدليل؛ لأنَّ دليلها عقلي؛ إما وحده، وإما مع النقلي على وجه التأكيد، ودليله نقلي إما وحده أو مع العقلي على وجه التأكيد؛ فالمعول عليه فيه الدليل السمعي كما سيذكره بقوله : (بذي أتانا السمع).
His saying: (Likewise Speech) – (Likewise) is a forward predicate, and (Speech) is a delayed subject. The meaning: Speech is like this; i.e., like the previous attributes in the comparison, not in every respect but in absolute necessity for God Most High, even though it differs from them in evidence. Because their evidence is rational – either alone or with transmitted evidence for emphasis – and its evidence is transmitted – either alone or with rational evidence for emphasis. So the reliance in it is on transmitted evidence (al-dalīl al-samʿī), as he will mention in his saying: (By that which hearing brought us).
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the line "كَذَا الكَلامُ" from the poem. The commentator clarifies the syntax and meaning: "كَذَا" is a predicate placed before the subject "الكَلامُ", and the overall meaning is that the attribute of Speech is obligatory for God Most High, just like the previously mentioned attributes (such as Power, Will, etc.), but it differs from them in terms of the evidence that establishes it. The evidence for the earlier attributes is primarily rational (ʿaqlī), though it may be accompanied by transmitted evidence for emphasis. In contrast, the evidence for the attribute of Speech is primarily transmitted (naqlī), though it may be accompanied by rational evidence for emphasis. The commentator then notes that the author will later point to this transmitted evidence in the line "بذي أتانا السمع" (by that which hearing brought us), indicating that revelation is the main source for establishing this attribute.

Key Terms
صفة الكلام
الدليل السمعي
الدليل العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All divine attributes are proven by the same type of evidence, either all rational or all transmitted.
Correction: This passage shows that while many attributes have rational evidence as primary, the attribute of Speech has transmitted evidence as primary. The type of evidence can differ.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the primary evidence for the attribute of Speech?
A) Rational evidence alone
B) Transmitted evidence (al-dalīl al-samʿī)
C) Both rational and transmitted equally
D) Empirical observation

Key Takeaway
The attribute of God's Speech is primarily proven by transmitted evidence (revelation), not by rational proof alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Interpreting Ambiguous Verses: Salaf and Khalaf Approaches
Commentator explains 'awwilhu aw fawwid' and compares Salaf and Khalaf methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two methods of dealing with ambiguous Qur'anic verses: detailed interpretation (ta'wil tafsili) of the Khalaf and consignment (tafwid) of the Salaf, and their agreement on summary interpretation.

Core Idea
Both Salaf and Khalaf first divert the apparent meaning, but differ on specifying it.

When we encounter Qur'anic verses that seem to imply something unbefitting of Allah, scholars have two main approaches. The commentator explains the line from the poem 'awwilhu aw fawwid'—either interpret it with detail or consign its meaning. This lesson unpacks these methods, highlighting how both schools agree on the first step but diverge thereafter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the key terms 'ta'wil tafsili' and 'tafwid'.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two methods
Look for how the commentator defines 'awwilhu' (detailed interpretation) and 'fawwid' (consignment). Note the groups associated with each.
Khalaf = later scholars; Salaf = early scholars.
3
Understand the comparison
The commentator evaluates the two methods: Khalaf's is 'more knowledgeable' and 'more judicious'; Salaf's is 'safer'. Ask yourself why each has that advantage.
The author of the poem gave precedence to the Khalaf method.
4
Grasp the common ground
The passage ends by stating that both groups agree on 'summary interpretation' (ta'wil ijmaili). This is the key point of unity.
The disagreement is only after that first step.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : (أَوَّلُهُ) ؛ أي: احمله على خلاف ظاهره مع بيان المعنى المراد، فالمراد: أوّله تاويلاً تفصيليا بأن يكون فيه بيان المعنى المراد كما هو مذهب الخلف وهم من بعد الخمس مئة ، وقيل : من بعد القرون الثلاثة .
وقوله : أَوْ فَوِّضُ ؛ أي: بعد التأويل الإجمالي الذي هو صرف اللفظ عن ظاهره ؛ فبعد هذا التأويل فوّض المراد من النص الموهم إليه تعالى على طريقة السلفِ، وهم من كانوا قبل الخمس مئة ، وقيل : القرون الثلاثة الصحابة والتابعون وأتباع التابعين وطريقة الخلفِ أعلمُ وأحكمُ ؛ لما فيها من مزيد الإيضاح والرد على الخصوم، وهي الأرجح، ولذلك قدَّمها المصنّف ، وطريقة السلف أسلم؛ لما فيها من السلامة من تعيين معنى قد يكون غير مرادٍ له تعالى.
وقوله : (وَرُمْ تَنْزِيهَا أي: واقصد تنزيهاً له تعالى عما لا يليقُ به مع تفويض علم المعنى المراد ) ، فظهر ممَّا قررناه اتفاق السلف والخلف على التأويل الإجمالي؛ لأنَّهم يصرفون النصَّ الموهم عن ظاهره المحال عليه تعالى، لكنهم اختلفوا بعد ذلك في تعيين المراد من ذلك النصّ وعدم التعيين
And his saying: 'Interpret it (awwilhu)' means: carry it to contrary of its apparent meaning with clarification of the intended meaning. So the intended meaning is: interpret it with detailed interpretation (ta'wil tafsili) such that there is clarification of the intended meaning, as is the school of the Khalaf (later scholars), who are those after five hundred [AH], and it is said: after the three generations. And his saying: 'or consign it (aw fawwid)' means: after the summary interpretation (ta'wil ijmaili) which is diverting the word from its apparent meaning; so after this interpretation, consign the intended meaning of the text that gives a false impression with respect to Him, Most High, according to the method of the Salaf, who are those before five hundred [AH], and it is said: the three generations: the Companions, the Successors, and the followers of the Successors. The method of the Khalaf is more knowledgeable and more judicious because of the greater clarification and refutation of opponents it contains, and it is the more preponderant, so the author gave it precedence. The method of the Salaf is safer because of the safety from specifying a meaning that may not be intended for Him, Most High. And his saying: 'and intend its transcendence (wa rum tanziha)' means: and intend transcendence for Him, Most High, from what is not befitting for Him while consigning knowledge of the intended meaning. So it becomes clear from what we have established that there is agreement between Salaf and Khalaf on summary interpretation (ta'wil ijmaili) because they turn the text that gives a false impression from its apparent meaning which is impossible for Him, Most High. However, they differed after that regarding specifying the intended meaning from that text or not specifying it.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage explains the line from the poem 'awwilhu aw fawwid wa rum tanziha'. The commentator begins by defining 'awwilhu' (interpret it) as carrying the word to a meaning contrary to its apparent sense while clarifying the intended meaning. This is the detailed interpretation (ta'wil tafsili) favored by the Khalaf (later scholars, after 500 AH or after the first three generations). Then he defines 'aw fawwid' (or consign it) as first performing a summary interpretation (ta'wil ijmaili) that diverts the word from its apparent meaning, and then consigning the actual intended meaning to Allah, following the method of the Salaf (early scholars, before 500 AH or the first three generations). The commentator notes that the Khalaf method is more knowledgeable (a'lam) and more judicious (ahkam) because it provides clearer explanation and refutes opponents, and thus is preponderant (arjah) – which is why the author gave it precedence. The Salaf method is safer (aslam) because it avoids assigning a meaning that might not be intended by Allah. The passage then states that both schools agree on the necessity of summary interpretation – turning the text from its impossible apparent meaning – but they differ on whether to specify the intended meaning after that. This highlights the key difference between the two approaches.

Key Terms
تأويل إجمالي
تأويل تفصيلي
تفويض
السلف
الخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Salaf reject all interpretation of ambiguous verses.
Correction: The commentator shows that both Salaf and Khalaf perform summary interpretation (ta'wil ijmaili) to avoid the impossible apparent meaning. The Salaf only stop short of specifying the exact intended meaning, but they do interpret in a general sense.

Checkpoint
What is the main difference between the Salaf and Khalaf methods according to the passage?
A) The Salaf reject interpretation entirely, while the Khalaf accept it.
B) The Salaf perform only summary interpretation and consign the meaning, while the Khalaf perform detailed interpretation and specify the meaning.
C) The Salaf always take the literal meaning, while the Khalaf always allegorize.
D) The Salaf and Khalaf have identical methods.

Key Takeaway
Both Salaf and Khalaf agree on diverting ambiguous verses from their impossible apparent meaning, but the Khalaf specify the intended meaning while the Salaf consign it to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse about Allah that initially troubles you, do you rush to find an explanation (like the Khalaf) or do you entrust the meaning to Allah (like the Salaf)? Which response reflects your current relationship with uncertainty and trust in Allah?
?
The commentator says the Khalaf method is 'more knowledgeable' but the Salaf method is 'safer'. In your own spiritual life, are you more driven by the pursuit of knowledge and clarity, or by the fear of saying something wrong about Allah? What is the cost of your priority?
?
Both groups agree that ambiguous verses must be turned from their impossible apparent meaning. Have you ever insisted on a literal reading of a verse that led to an unworthy understanding of Allah? What would it take for you to accept that your initial understanding required correction?

Why Scholars Interpret Prophets' Words Over Saints'
Al-Sha'rani asks al-Khawwas about interpreting ambiguous statements of prophets vs. saints.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why scholars prioritize interpreting ambiguous statements of prophets over those of saints, based on the principle of following (iqtida').

Core Idea
Scholars preserve the Prophet's words because he is followed; saints' words may be overlooked.

In this anecdote, al-Sha'rani poses a question that gets at a key methodological distinction in Islamic scholarship. The response reveals a practical principle: the words of the Lawgiver must be preserved and interpreted because the community follows them, whereas saints, though honored, are not sources of binding law. This lesson explains the reasoning behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the dialogue.
Notice the two answers given by al-Khawwas.
2
Identify the two answers
The first answer appeals to fairness: the saint is more excused due to spiritual states. The second answer is pragmatic: the Prophet is followed, the saint is not.
3
Reflect on the principle
Consider why the second answer (following) is the operative principle in Islamic methodology. Think about how this affects the treatment of primary vs. secondary sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لطيفة : سأل الشعراني شيخه الخواص : لماذا يؤول العلماء الموهم الواقع من الشارع، ولا يؤولون الموهم الواقع مِنَ الولي؟ فقال: لو أنصفوا لأوّلوا الواقع من الولي بالأولى؛ لأنه معذور بضعفه في أحوال الحضرة، بخلاف الشارع فإنه ذو مقام مكين. وقد يُقال : الشارع ينبغي المحافظة على الواقع منه ما أمكن؛ لأنَّه يُقتدى به، ولا كذلك الولي؛ فإنه لا يحافظ على كلامه؛ لأنه لا يُقتدى به، فإذا أوهم أهدر.
A subtle point: Al-Sha'rani asked his teacher al-Khawwas: Why do scholars interpret the ambiguous statement occurring from the Lawgiver, but they do not interpret the ambiguous statement occurring from the saint? He said: If they were fair, they would interpret the statement occurring from the saint first, because he is excused due to his weakness in the states of the [divine] presence, unlike the Lawgiver, for he possesses a firm station. And it may be said: The Lawgiver — one should preserve as much as possible what occurs from him, because he is followed; but the saint is not like that, for he does not preserve his speech because he is not followed. So if he is ambiguous, he is disregarded.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This is a latifa (subtle anecdote) in which al-Sha'rani asks al-Khawwas why scholars go to great lengths to interpret ambiguous statements from the Prophet (the Lawgiver) but not from saints. Al-Khawwas gives two answers. First, that ideally scholars should interpret the saint's words even more, because the saint is excused by his spiritual state (ahwal al-hadra) while the Prophet is firm. But then a second, more pragmatic reason is given: the Prophet's words must be preserved because he is followed (yuqtada bihi), whereas saints are not necessarily followed, so if their statements are ambiguous, they can be disregarded. This highlights an important principle in traditional scholarship: the priority of interpreting the primary sources (Qur'an and Sunnah) over the words of later figures.

Key Terms
لطيفة
شارع
ولي
تأويل
موهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Saints' statements are given the same interpretative effort as the Prophet's.
Correction: This anecdote clarifies that saints' ambiguous statements are not necessarily interpreted because they are not followed as a source of law.

Checkpoint
According to the anecdote, why do scholars interpret the ambiguous statements of the Prophet but not of saints?
A) The Prophet is more knowledgeable.
B) The Prophet is followed and his words are preserved.
C) Saints never make ambiguous statements.
D) Scholars dislike saints.

Key Takeaway
Scholars prioritize interpreting the Prophet's ambiguous words over saints' because the Prophet is followed by the community, making his words a source of law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear an ambiguous statement from a spiritual guide or scholar, do you hold them to the same standard of clarity as you do the Prophet? How might this lesson reveal your unconscious tendency to follow people rather than the Message?
?
The lesson teaches that saints are excused due to weakness in divine presence. Think of a time you judged someone's mistake harshly. Will you now extend the same fairness to them that al-Khawwas suggested for saints?
?
Are you someone whom others look up to? If so, do you guard your words carefully, knowing that your ambiguous statements could lead others astray? What specific steps will you take to ensure your speech is clear and upright?

Why Not Interpret the Saint's Apparent Statements?
A story and two answers on interpreting problematic statements from saints vs. the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the methodological reasoning behind why scholars interpret the Prophet's ambiguous statements but not those of saints, and the two justifications provided by al-Khawas.

Core Idea
Saints are excused; Prophets are followed.

This lesson presents a subtle point (lathīfa) from the tradition of Islamic scholarship. It addresses a practical question of how to handle problematic statements from saints compared to the Prophet. The answer reveals important principles about the authority of prophetic speech and the leniency afforded to saints due to their spiritual states.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the story.
Notice the question and the two responses given by al-Khawas.
2
Identify the question
Al-Sha'rani asks why scholars interpret (ta'wīl) the Prophet's apparent problematic statements but not those of saints. Understand the term 'muwahhim' – something that gives a problematic impression.
3
Examine the first answer
Al-Khawas says that if scholars were fair, they would interpret the saint's words even more because the saint is excused due to weakness in the state of divine presence (ḥāl al-ḥaḍra). The Prophet, however, has a firm station.
This answer focuses on leniency for the saint's spiritual state.
4
Examine the second answer
The second answer shifts perspective: the Prophet's words are preserved because he is followed (uqtada bihi), while saints are not followed, so their problematic statements can be disregarded.
This answer highlights the practical consequence of being an exemplar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لطيفة : سأل الشعراني شيخه الخواص : لماذا يؤول العلماء الموهم الواقع من الشارع، ولا يؤولون الموهم الواقع مِنَ الولي؟ فقال: لو أنصفوا لأوّلوا الواقع من الولي بالأولى؛ لأنه معذور بضعفه في أحوال الحضرة، بخلاف الشارع فإنه ذو مقام مكين. وقد يُقال : الشارع ينبغي المحافظة على الواقع منه ما أمكن؛ لأنَّه يُقتدى به، ولا كذلك الولي؛ فإنه لا يحافظ على كلامه؛ لأنه لا يُقتدى به، فإذا أوهم أهدر.
A subtle point: Al-Sha'rani asked his teacher al-Khawas: Why do the scholars interpret the apparent (text) that occurs from the Lawgiver (Prophet), and they do not interpret the apparent that occurs from the saint? He said: If they were fair, they would interpret the apparent from the saint even more; because he is excused due to his weakness in the states of the divine presence, unlike the Lawgiver, for he possesses a firm station. And it may be said: It is appropriate to preserve the apparent from the Lawgiver as much as possible, because he is followed, and the saint is not so; indeed he does not preserve his speech because he is not followed; so if something apparently problematic occurs, it is disregarded.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a story (lathīfa) that answers a methodological question: why do scholars interpret (ta’wīl) problematic expressions from the Prophet but not from saints? Al-Khawas gives two answers. First, if they were fair, they would interpret the saint’s words even more, because the saint is excused due to weakness in the divine presence, whereas the Prophet has a firm station. Second, it is better to preserve the apparent meaning from the Prophet because he is followed; saints are not followed, so their problematic utterances can be ignored.

Key Terms
الولي
الشارع
المؤول
الموهم
الحضرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The saint is not excused because of weakness; rather his words are not followed, so problematic statements can be ignored.
Correction: The passage presents both excuses: the saint is excused due to weakness and also not followed, so his problematic statements are not given the same interpretive effort.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it not necessary to interpret the apparent problematic statements of a saint?
A) Because saints are infallible
B) Because saints are not followed
C) Because saints do not make mistakes
D) Because saints are excused for weakness

Key Takeaway
Scholars interpret the Prophet's words to protect his example, but saints' words are not followed, so they can be excused or disregarded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Interpreting Texts That Indicate the Quran's Createdness
Hermeneutic rule for Quranic creation texts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to interpret Quranic and hadith texts that appear to imply the Quran is created, by applying the principle that such texts refer to the recited word (al-lafẓ) rather than the eternal attribute.

Core Idea
Texts about the Quran's createdness refer to the revealed word, not the eternal attribute.

The Mu'tazila used verses like 'Indeed, We have sent down the Reminder' (15:9) to argue that the Quran is created. This passage provides a hermeneutic rule: any such text must be interpreted as referring to the 'word' (al-lafẓ)—the Quran sent down to the Prophet—not to Allah's eternal speech. The commentator clarifies the grammar and context of this rule.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Let the commentator's flow come through.
The Arabic text ends abruptly due to a page break; the meaning is clear up to that point.
2
Identify the core rule
Find the line of poetry and its commentary: 'every text that indicates origination, interpret it as referring to the word.'
This is the hermeneutic principle.
3
Understand the context
Note that this rule is a direct response to the Mu'tazila's proof from texts like Quran 15:9.
The fa' of 'fakullu' is called 'fa' al-fasiḥa' indicating a conclusion.
4
Define key terms
See what 'text' (naṣṣ) and 'word' (lafẓ) mean: text is the apparent meaning from Quran or Sunnah; word is the revealed recited Quran.
The preponderant view is that both word and meaning were sent down.
5
Reflect on theological significance
Consider why this matters: it protects the belief that Allah's attribute of speech is eternal and uncreated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فَكُلُّ نَصَّ لِلْحُدُوثِ دَلَّا اِحْمِلْ عَلَى اللَّفْظِ الَّذِي قَدْ دَلَّا) أي : إذا تحققت ما سبق فكل نص .. إلى آخره، فالفاء فاء الفصيحة، وهذا في الحقيقة جواب عمّا تمسَّكَ به المعتزلة من النصوص الدالّة على الحدوث مثل: إِنَّا نَحْنُ نَزَّلْنَا الذِكْرَ ﴾ [الحجر: (٩، والمراد من النص: الظاهر من الكتاب أو السنة. وقوله : (لِلْحُدُوثِ دَلَّا) أي دلّ على حدوث ،القرآن فاللام بمعنى على، والألف في (دلا) للإطلاق. وقوله : (اِحْمِلْ ... إلخ) خبر المبتدأ الذي هو (كل)، والرابط محذوف، والتقدير : (احمله ... إلخ). وقوله : (عَلَى اللَّفْظِ) أي: على القرآن بمعنى اللفظ المنزل على نبينا ، المتعبد بتلاوته المتحدي بأقصر سورة منه، والراجح أن المنزَّلَ اللفظ والمعنى ، وقيل : المنزَّلُ
His saying: 'So every text that indicates origination — interpret it as referring to the word that has indicated [it]' — i.e., when you have understood what came before, then every text... etc. So the letter fa' is the fa' of eloquent speech, and this is in reality a response to the proof of the Mu'tazila from the texts that indicate origination, such as 'Indeed, We have sent down the Reminder' [Quran 15:9]. And what is meant by 'text' is the apparent meaning of the Book or the Sunna. And his saying: 'indicates origination' means it indicates the origination (huduth) of the Quran — so the lam is in the sense of 'upon', and the alif in 'dala' is for absolute [inflection]. And his saying: 'interpret it' is the predicate of the subject 'every', and the connector is omitted, the estimation being: 'interpret it... etc.' And his saying: 'upon the word' means upon the Quran in the sense of the word sent down to our Prophet, which we are obliged to recite and which challenges with the shortest surah thereof, and the preponderant view is that what was sent down is the word and the meaning, and it is said that what was sent down is...
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage explains a line of poetry that gives a hermeneutic rule: any text (from the Quran or Hadith) that appears to indicate that the Quran is originated (حدوث) must be interpreted as referring to the 'word' (اللفظ) — i.e., the recited Quran that was sent down to the Prophet, not the eternal attribute. The commentator clarifies that this is a direct response to the Mu'tazila who use verses like 'Indeed, We have sent down the Reminder' (15:9) as proof that the Quran is created. The grammatical analysis follows: 'so every text' (فكل نص) — the fa' is for eloquent speech; 'that indicates origination' (للحدوث دلا) — 'dala' with alif for absolute form; 'interpret it upon the word' (احمل على اللفظ). The 'word' (اللفظ) is specified as the Quran in the sense of the revealed text, which we recite and which is miraculous by challenging with the shortest surah. The commentator notes that the preponderant view is that both the word (اللفظ) and the meaning (المعنى) were sent down, though some say only the meaning was sent down. This principle protects the belief that the eternal attribute of Allah is uncreated.

Key Terms
نص
الحدوث
اللفظ
المنزل
المعتزلة
الكتاب والسنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Verses like 'We have sent down the Reminder' prove the Quran is created.
Correction: Such texts refer to the revealed word (al-lafẓ), not to the eternal attribute of speech, so they do not imply creation of the divine attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the rule 'every text that indicates origination, interpret it as referring to the word'?
A) To affirm the Quran is created
B) To refute the Mu'tazila's proof from those texts
C) To deny the revelation of the Quran
D) To ignore the verses

Key Takeaway
The principle of interpreting texts about the Quran's origination as referring to the recited word safeguards the eternal nature of Allah's speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Grammar and Theology of 'لعبده و ما عمل'
Grammatical and theological commentary on 'لعبده و ما عمل'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical analysis of the phrase 'لعبده و ما عمل' and how it refutes the Mu'tazilite position on human action.

Core Idea
Grammatical analysis of 'ما' refutes Mu'tazilite belief in human creation of actions.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the grammatical layers of a key phrase from the poem, drawing on the Qur'anic verse {والله خلقكم وما تعملون}. By examining two possible grammatical roles of 'ما', the author shows how the text itself undermines the Mu'tazilite claim that humans create their own voluntary actions. Let's explore this concise yet theologically rich analysis.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Read the passage noticing that it explains two words from the poem: 'لعبده' and 'وما عمل'. The author first clarifies the meaning of 'العبد' and then dives into the grammar of 'ما'.
2
Examine the two interpretations of 'ما'
The author presents two possibilities: 'ما' as masdariyya (making a verbal noun) and 'ما' as mawqula (relative pronoun). Assess which is preferred and why.
The first interpretation avoids omission (حذف), which is the principle.
3
Connect to the theological refutation
The author states that both interpretations apply to the Qur'anic verse and that this verse refutes the Mu'tazila. Ask yourself how the grammatical reading supports the doctrine that Allah creates all actions.
The verse attributes creation of both the person and their actions to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لِعَبْدِهِ) اللام للتقوية؛ والمراد من العبد كل مخلوق يصدر عنه الفعل عاقلاً كان أو غيره، خلافاً لبعضهم حيث قصَرَه على المكلَّف؛ لأنَّ بعض الأدلة التي ذكروها لا تجري في غير فعله، وإنما ذكر المصنف العبد مع أنه متفق على خلق الله إيَّاه توصلاً لما بعده، واقتداءً بقوله تعالى : ﴿ وَالله خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ ﴾ [الصافات: ٩٦] . وقوله : (وَمَا عَمِلْ) معطوف على عبده، و(ما) مصدرية، فيؤول الفعل بعدها بمصدر، والتقدير : فخالق لعبده ولعمله، ويحتمل أن تكون موصولة، و(عمل) صلة، والعائد محذوف، وعليه فالتقدير : فخالق لعبده وللذي عمله، والأول أولى؛ لأنَّه لا حذف عليه، والأصل عدم الحذف. ويجري الاحتمالان المذكوران في قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَاللهُ خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ ﴾ [الصافات: ٩٦]، وفي ذلك ردُّ على المعتزلة في قولهم : بأن العبد يخلق أفعال نفسه الاختياريَّة، وأما الأفعال الاضطراريَّة ؛ كحركة المرتعش فهي مخلوقة الله تعالى اتفاقاً .
His statement: (لِعَبْدِهِ) the لام is for strengthening; and the meaning of 'the servant' is every creation from whom an action emanates, whether rational or otherwise, contrary to some who restricted it to the legally responsible, because some of the evidences they mentioned do not apply to other than his action. The author mentioned 'the servant' despite the agreement that God creates him, as a means to what follows, and following the saying of Allah: {And Allah created you and what you do} [Surah As-Saffat: 96]. And his statement: (وَمَا عَمِل) is conjoined to 'his servant', and 'ما' is masdariyya, so the verb after it becomes a verbal noun, and the estimation: So He is the Creator of his servant and of his action. It is possible that it is mawqula, and 'عمل' is the relative clause, and the relative pronoun is omitted, and upon that the estimation: So He is the Creator of his servant and of that which he did. The first is more appropriate because there is no omission, and the principle is no omission. Both probabilities apply in the saying of Allah: {And Allah created you and what you do}, and in that is a refutation of the Mu'tazila in their statement that the servant creates his own voluntary actions. As for involuntary actions; like the movement of the trembling person, they are created by Allah by agreement.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage provides a grammatical and theological commentary on the word 'لعبده' and 'وما عمل' in the poem. First, the author explains that 'اللام' in 'لعبده' is for emphasis, and 'العبد' here includes all creatures capable of action, not just morally responsible humans. The author notes that the mention of 'servant' even though all agree God creates him, is to connect with the following discussion and to emulate the Qur'anic verse {والله خلقكم وما تعملون}. Then, he discusses the word 'وما عمل' as being conjoined to 'عبده'. He gives two possible grammatical interpretations of 'ما': as 'masdariyya' (making the phrase a verbal noun) or as 'mawqula' (meaning 'that which'). The preferred interpretation is the first because it avoids omission. He then points out that both interpretations apply to the Qur'anic verse itself, and that this verse refutes the Mu'tazilite position that humans create their own voluntary actions. Finally, he notes that involuntary actions (like the tremor of a shivering person) are agreed to be created by God.

Key Terms
اللام للتقوية
ما مصدرية
ما موصولة
أفعال اضطرارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'ما' in such constructions is always mawqula (relative pronoun).
Correction: The author shows it can be masdariyya (verbal noun) and that interpretation is preferred.

Checkpoint

A) Masdariyya and mawqula
B) Istifhamiyya and nafiya
C) Masdariyya and sharatiyya
D) Mawsula and nafiya

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic verse {والله خلقكم وما تعملون} refutes the Mu'tazilite position by attributing creation of both the servant and his actions to Allah, as grammatically supported by the preferred interpretation of 'ما' as masdariyya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does this lesson change the way you will attribute your successes? Will you consciously thank Allah for every action, big or small, starting now?

Have you ever harbored a similar doubt in your heart, even subconsciously? After reading this passage, do you still hold that objection? If not, what specific words from the source convinced you? If yes, what more do you need to surrender your understanding to the clear text?
?
The author preferred the interpretation of 'ما' as masdariyya because it involves no omission (حذف). Are you willing to accept the straightforward reading of the Qur'an even when it challenges your sense of autonomy? What is one area of your life where you act as if you are the creator of your own deeds? Name it honestly. Now, how will you submit that area to Allah's sole creative power before you sleep tonight?

The Two Testimonies Encompass All Beliefs
The author explains how the two shahadas include all Islamic beliefs and interprets the first shahada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the two testimonies encompass all Islamic beliefs through their meaning, and the distinction between the literal meaning of the first testimony and its necessary implication according to Shaykh al-Sanusi.

Core Idea
The shahada's meaning, not its wording, encompasses all beliefs.

This lesson examines a key passage from Tuhfat al-Mureed that explains why the two testimonies of Islam are comprehensive. The author clarifies that it is the meaning of the testimonies that gathers all beliefs, and then he dissects the first testimony to show its literal meaning and its necessary implications, which include Allah's self-sufficiency and the dependence of all else upon Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the discussion about 'addition' and the two testimonies.
Pay attention to the terms 'istighna' (self-sufficiency) and 'iftiqar' (dependence).
2
Identify the two types of 'idafa'
The author mentions two types of grammatical addition (cause-to-effect and part-to-whole) in reference to prophecies in Islam. Understand this as a methodological point.
3
Understand the centrality of meaning
The author states that the meaning of the two testimonies, not their wording, unites all beliefs. Reflect on why this distinction matters.
This counters a possible misconception that the mere utterance of the shahada suffices.
4
Analyze the first testimony
The author breaks down 'La ilaha illa Allah' into its true meaning (no deity worshipped by right except Allah) and its necessary implication (Allah is self-sufficient, all else depends on Him). Notice how Shaykh al-Sanusi chose the implication for his explanation.
The implication is chosen because it more clearly shows the entailment of other beliefs.
5
Reflect on the completeness
Ask yourself why this analysis is foundational. How does understanding the first testimony as implying self-sufficiency and dependence lead to other beliefs about Allah and creation?
This is the beginning of a systematic derivation of Islamic creed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النبوات في الإسلام، فالإضافة في كلامه من إضافة السبب للمسبب، أو اللتان هما الجزء الأعظم من مُسمَّى الإسلام، بناءً على أنه الهيئة المركبة من الأركان الخمسة المذكورة في حديث: «بني الإسلام على خمس»، فالإضافة في كلامه من إضافة الجزء للكل. والجامع لما تقدَّم من العقائد إنَّما هو معنى الشهادتين لا لفظهما، فكلام المصنف على حذف مضاف؛ أي معنى شهادتي الإسلام كما أشار إليه الشارح، ومعنى جمعه لها استلزامه لها؛ لأن الملزوم يصح وصفه بجمعه للوازمه بالنظر لدلالته عليها وقوله: (فَاطْرَحِ المِرَاءَ) تكملةٌ؛ أي: إذا علمت أن كلمتي الشهادتين جمعتا جميع ما تقرر من العقائد الإيمانية، فاترك الجدال في صحة جمعهما لما ذكر. وبيان ما ذكره أن الجملة الأولى نفتِ الألوهية عن غيره تعالى، وأثبتتها له، وحقيقة الألوهية العبادة بحقِّ، ويلزم منها استغناء الإله عن كل ما سواه، وافتقار كلّ ما عداه إليه، فحقيقة الإله المعبود بحقِّ، ويلزم منه أنه مُستغن عن كل ما سواه، ومفتقر إليه كلُّ ما عداه فمعنى لا إله إلا الله الحقيقي: لا معبود بحق في الواقع إلا الله، ومعناها بطريق اللزوم: لا مُستغنياً عن كلِّ ما سواه، ومُفتقراً إليه كلُّ ما عداه إلا الله، فتفسير الشيخ السنوسي الذي ذكره في الصغرى باللازم لا بالحقيقة. وإنما اختاره لكون استلزامه للعقائد المتقدّمة أظهر من استلزام المعنى الحقيقي لها.
The prophecies in Islam, so the addition in his speech is of the type attribution of cause to effect, or the two which are the greatest part of the name 'Islam', based on it being the composite form of the five pillars mentioned in the hadith: 'Islam is built on five', so the addition in his speech is of the type attribution of the part to the whole. And the comprehensive of what has preceded of beliefs is only the meaning of the two testimonies, not their wording, so the author's speech is by elision of a genitive; i.e. the meaning of the two testimonies of Islam as the commentator indicated, and the meaning of his gathering them is their entailment; because the entailed thing is correctly described as gathering its entailments in view of its indication of them. And his saying: 'So abandon argument' is a completion; i.e. when you know that the two words of the testimonies have gathered all of what has been established of the beliefs of faith, so abandon disputation about the correctness of their gathering what was mentioned. And an explanation of what he mentioned is that the first sentence negated divinity from other than Him, may He be exalted, and affirmed it for Him, and the reality of divinity is worship by right, and from it follows the self-sufficiency of the deity from everything other than Him, and the dependence of everything other than Him upon Him. So the reality of the deity is the one worshipped by right, and from it follows that He is self-sufficient from everything other than Him, and everything other than Him is dependent upon Him. So the true meaning of 'there is no god but Allah' is: no one is worshipped by right in actuality except Allah, and its meaning by way of entailment is: there is none self-sufficient from everything other than Him, and none upon whom everything other than Him is dependent, except Allah. So the interpretation of Shaykh al-Sanusi which he mentioned in al-Sughra is by the necessary implication, not by the true meaning. And he only chose it because its entailment of the preceding beliefs is more apparent than the entailment of the true meaning of them.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of how the two testimonies (الشهادتين) encompass all Islamic beliefs. The author begins by discussing the phrase 'النبوات في الإسلام' (the prophecies in Islam), clarifying that the addition (إضافة) in the author's words is of the type cause to effect, or part to whole, referring to the hadith that Islam is built on five pillars. He then asserts that what brings together all previous beliefs is the meaning of the two testimonies, not their mere wording. The author's statement 'فاطرح المِرَاء' (so abandon argument) is a completion, urging the reader to cease disputing once they understand that the testimonies encompass all faith-based beliefs. The author then explains the first testimony (لا إله إلا الله) in detail. He states that its true meaning (المعنى الحقيقي) is that no one is truly worshipped except Allah, and from this meaning, two necessary implications (لازم) follow: the self-sufficiency (الاستغناء) of Allah from everything else, and the dependence (الافتقار) of everything else upon Him. He notes that Imam al-Sanusi, in his work al-Sughra, interpreted the testimony by its necessary implication rather than its literal meaning, because the implication makes its entailment of the subsequent beliefs more evident. This sets the stage for the detailed derivation of beliefs in the next lesson.

Key Terms
الشهادتين
الاستغناء
الافتقار
اللزوم
الحقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the shahada is merely verbal and its recitation alone includes all beliefs.
Correction: The author emphasizes that it is the meaning of the two testimonies, not the wording, that encompasses all beliefs. The meaning entails necessary implications such as Allah's self-sufficiency and creation's dependence.

Checkpoint
What is the difference between the true meaning (al-haqiqi) of 'La ilaha illa Allah' and its interpretation by necessary implication (al-luzum) according to Shaykh al-Sanusi?
A) The true meaning affirms divinity for Allah, while the necessary implication negates it.
B) The true meaning is about worship, while the necessary implication adds the attributes of self-sufficiency and dependence.
C) There is no difference.
D) The necessary implication is about the second testimony.

Key Takeaway
The meaning of the two testimonies, not their wording, encompasses all beliefs, and the first testimony implies Allah's self-sufficiency and creation's dependence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Lā ilāha illā Allāh', do you truly feel in your heart that it affirms Allah's total self-sufficiency and your own complete dependence? If not, what keeps you from feeling that reality?
?
The author says the meaning of the shahada encompasses all beliefs. Which of your current beliefs or practices can you now trace back to the shahada? How does that awareness change how you hold that belief?
?
If you truly internalized that the shahada implies all beliefs, what arguments or disputes with other Muslims would you have to abandon because they are settled by the shahada? How would that change your humility?

Analyzing the Metaphor of the Mountain Pass
Commentary on a metaphor comparing difficult worship to a mountain pass.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator analyzes a metaphor (istiʿāra) in a poetic line from the Jawharat al-Tawḥīd, identifying its elements: the thing compared, the thing compared to, the common factor, and the rhetorical devices used.

Core Idea
The hardest worship is like climbing a steep mountain pass — both require extreme hardship.

In this lesson, we examine a short but rich passage where the commentator unpacks a single poetic line. The poet uses a vivid metaphor to convey the difficulty of certain acts of worship. The commentator shows us how this metaphor works by breaking it down into its components: the borrowed word, the common factor, and the reinforcing word that strengthens the image.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage from the matn and the literal translation. Let the words settle before analysis.
2
Identify the two things being compared
Notice that the commentator explicitly states the comparison: the most difficult acts of worship are likened to the highest mountain pass. Look for the phrase 'shabbaha ashqa al-ʿibādāt bi-aʿlā ʿaqaba'.
3
Find the common factor (jāmiʿ)
The shared element is hardship (mashaqqa). Both climbing a steep pass and performing hard worship involve great difficulty.
4
Recognize the type of metaphor and reinforcement
This is an explicit original metaphor (istiʿāra taṣrīḥiyya aṣliyya). The word 'raqā' (ascended) reinforces the metaphor by fitting the image of climbing.
5
Reflect on the commentator's method
Notice how the commentator systematically unpacks the figure of speech. This illustrates the importance of rhetorical analysis for understanding theological poetry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَلَو رَقَى فِي الخَيْرِ أَعْلَى عَقَبَهُ) أي: ولو فعل العبد في الخير أشقّ العبادات، فشبه أشقَّ العبادات بأعلى عقبة ; وهى فى الأصل الطريق الصاعد في الجبل بجامع المشقة في كلِّ، واستعير لفظ المشبه به للمشبَّه على طريق الاستعارة التصريحية الأصلية، و(رقى) ترشيح للاستعارة; لأن الرقيَّ معناه الصعود، وهو مناسب للمشبه به .
His saying: (And even if he ascended in goodness the highest mountain pass) means: even if the servant performed the most difficult acts of worship in goodness. So he likened the most difficult acts of worship to the highest mountain pass, which originally is the ascending path in the mountain, due to the common factor of hardship in each. The word of the thing likened (the act of worship) is borrowed for the thing it is likened to (the mountain pass) by way of original explicit metaphor (istiʿāra taṣrīḥiyya aṣliyya). And (ascended) is a reinforcement (tarshīḥ) of the metaphor because its meaning is climbing, which is appropriate to the thing it is likened to.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage analyzes a poetic line that says 'and even if he ascended in goodness the highest mountain pass.' The commentator explains that it means even if a person performs the most difficult acts of worship. The poet has made a metaphor (istiʿāra) by comparing the hardest worship to a high mountain pass (ʿaqaba), because both involve extreme hardship. The word 'ʿaqaba' originally means a steep mountain road. The comparison is based on the shared element of difficulty. The commentator specifies that this is an original explicit metaphor (istiʿāra taṣrīḥiyya aṣliyya), where the term for the thing being compared (the mountain pass) is used for the thing being described (the worship). The word 'raqā' (ascended) is a reinforcement (tarshīḥ) of the metaphor, because it fits the mountain climb. This shows the rhetorical skill of the poet and the commentator's method of explaining figurative language.

Key Terms
استعارة تصريحية
تشبيه
ترشيح
عقبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The line might be interpreted literally as climbing a mountain.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that it is a metaphor for exerting extreme effort in worship, not literal climbing.

Checkpoint
What is the 'common factor' (jāmiʿ) that links the act of worship to the mountain pass in this metaphor?
A) Height
B) Hardship
C) Speed
D) Destination

Key Takeaway
The commentator reveals that the poet's line uses an explicit metaphor where the hardship of worship is compared to climbing a steep mountain pass, reinforced by the word 'ascended'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face the most difficult acts of worship, do you see them as a steep mountain pass that demands your full effort? How does this metaphor change your attitude toward hardship in obedience?
?
The poet chose the 'highest' mountain pass. Are you seeking the highest level of effort in your worship, or settling for easier paths? What would it mean for you to 'ascend' in goodness starting today?
?
The commentator explains that the word 'ascended' reinforces the metaphor. In your life, what small actions (like a word of praise or a moment of patience) could reinforce your climb toward Allah, even when the path is steep?

The Meaning of Shiqaq and the Ruling on Disputing Consensus
Defines shiqaq and explains the ruling on disputing consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of shiqaq (dissension) and why disputing a consensus ruling is impermissible.

Core Idea
Shiqaq means each side stands apart; disputing consensus divides the community.

The text addresses a key principle in Islamic theology: once a ruling is established by consensus, it is not open to dispute. The poet uses the word 'shiqaq' to describe such disputation, and the commentator explains its literal meaning to emphasize the divisiveness of going against consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the phrase 'فَمِلْ عَنِ الشَّقَاقِ' and its connection to the ruling on consensus.
The passage is short, so take time to understand each word.
2
Understand the ruling on disputing consensus
The passage states that once a ruling is known to be agreed upon by consensus, it is not permissible to dispute it. This is because violating consensus is not allowed.
The commentator notes a reference to al-Zamakhshari, who is known for his rationalist interpretations.
3
Explore the etymology of shiqaq
The term shiqaq is derived from shiqq (side), because each disputant is on a side that the other is not on. This literal meaning reinforces the idea of division and opposition.
Understanding the root helps appreciate why the term is used for such disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فَمِلْ عَنِ الشَّقَاقِ) أي: إذا عرفت هذا الحكم المجمع عليه فاعدل عن المنازعة فيه؛ لأنه لا تجوز المنازعة في الحكم المجمع عليه؛ إذ لا يجوز خرقُ الإجماع، وقد أشار المصنف بذلك لمنازعة الزمخشري ، وإنما سميت المنازعة شقاقاً؛ لأن كُلًّا من المتنازعين يكون في شق أي جانب لا يكون فيه الآخر.
His saying: 'So turn away from dissension' means: When you know this agreed-upon ruling, then desist from disputing it, because disputing an agreed-upon ruling is not permissible, as violating consensus is not permissible. The author pointed thereby to the disputation of al-Zamakhshari. The dispute is called shiqaq because each of the disputants is in a side (shiqq) that the other is not in.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage explains the phrase 'فَمِلْ عَنِ الشَّقَاقِ' (so turn away from dissension) from the poem. It instructs that once a ruling is established by consensus, one must avoid disputing it, because it is impermissible to violate consensus. The author (the commentator) notes that this is a reference to the disputation of al-Zamakhshari, likely on a theological issue. The term shiqaq (dissension) is then etymologized: it derives from shiqq (side), because each disputant takes a side that the other does not occupy. This reinforces the idea that dissension is a division that should be avoided after consensus.

Key Terms
الشقاق
الإجماع
المنازعة
الزمخشري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Shiqaq is just a synonym for any disagreement.
Correction: Shiqaq specifically implies being on opposite sides, each refusing to come together, as the passage explains each disputant is in a side the other is not in.

Checkpoint
Why is the term 'shiqaq' used for dispute according to this passage?
A) Because it causes division.
B) Because each disputant is in a separate side.
C) Because it is forbidden.
D) Because it involves two parties.

Key Takeaway
Shiqaq (dissension) is called such because each disputant stands in a separate side; disputing consensus is not allowed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you disagree with a religious ruling that is widely accepted by scholars, do you immediately take a side and dig in, or do you pause and ask yourself if you are falling into shiqaq? Recall a specific instance. What would it take for you to 'turn away' from that dissension today?
?
The author warns against disputing consensus. Is there a matter of faith or practice where you know the majority of Muslims agree, yet you still argue against it? What is the pride or attachment in your heart that keeps you standing alone on your side?
?
Shiqaq means each person stands in a separate side. Which 'side' have you placed yourself on that isolates you from the community of believers? Are you willing to step into the middle ground of submission to consensus, even if it means admitting you were wrong?

Rulings on Denying Miracles Based on Certainty
Author classifies miracles by certainty and assigns ruling: kufr, fisq, or ta'zeer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars classify miracles according to the certainty of their transmission and the corresponding legal consequences for denying them.

Core Idea
Denying a miracle is not always disbelief; it depends on how the miracle is known.

The author of the Jawharat al-Tawhid now turns to a practical application of the principles of certainty. Not all miracles are transmitted with the same level of authority, so the ruling on denying them varies accordingly. In this brief but important passage, he lays out a three-tier classification that balances theological rigour with fairness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author uses a conditional structure: 'if this, then that' for each category.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three categories
Find the three levels of certainty: (1) known by tawatur, (2) mashhur (famous), (3) established by a sound/hasan chain but not famous.
Each category has a different ruling.
3
Match the rulings
For each category, note the ruling: kufr, fisq, ta'zeer. Understand that disbelief (kufr) is only for the highest level of certainty.
This shows a nuanced approach.
4
Reflect on the wisdom
Think about why the scholars graded these rulings. What does this teach us about the importance of certainty in matters of faith?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن ما كان منها معلوماً بالقطع منقولاً بالتواتر كالقرآن .. فلا شك في كفر منكره، وما لم يكن منها كذلك، فإن اشتهر كنبع الماء من بين أصابعه .. فسق منكره، وإن لم يشتهر وثبت بطريق صحيح أو حسن .. عُزّر منكره.
And know that whatever of them (the miracles) is known with certainty, transmitted by tawatur (mass transmission) like the Qur'an, then there is no doubt in the disbelief of its denier. And whatever is not like that, if it is famous (mashhur) like the gushing of water from between his fingers, then its denier is deemed corrupt (fasiq). And if it is not famous and is established by a sound or good chain, then its denier is to be disciplined (ta'zeer).
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of miracles based on the level of certainty in their transmission, with corresponding legal rulings. The author begins by stating the rule for miracles that are known by definitive proof (ma'lum bil-qat') and transmitted via mass transmission (tawatur), like the Qur'an. Denying such a miracle constitutes disbelief (kufr) without exception. Then he addresses miracles that are not at that level: if they are well-known (mashhur) among the community, like the miracle of water flowing from the Prophet's fingers, then denying them is considered corruption (fisq), not disbelief. Finally, for miracles that are less famous but still authenticated by a sound (sahih) or good (hasan) chain of narration, the denier receives a discretionary punishment (ta'zeer). This gradation demonstrates the nuanced approach of the scholars in assessing reactions to narrated miracles.

Key Terms
معلوم بالقطع
متواتر
مشهور
فسق
تعزير
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying any authentic miracle is kufr.
Correction: Only miracles known by tawatur lead to kufr; less certain miracles may only entail fisq or ta'zeer.

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on denying a miracle that is known by tawatur?
A) Ta'zeer
B) Fisq
C) Kufr
D) No ruling

Key Takeaway
The ruling on denying a miracle depends on the level of certainty in its transmission; only those known by mass transmission constitute disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a miracle from Islamic tradition that you have heard about but never verified the level of its transmission. Based on this lesson, how should your attitude toward it differ from your attitude toward the Qur'an? Are you treating them with the same level of certainty?
?
This lesson warns against hastily declaring someone a kafir for denying a miracle that is not mutawatir. Is there someone you have judged or distanced yourself from for questioning a religious story? How does this classification change that judgment?
?
Consider the category of miracles that are sahih but not famous: the author says denying them warrants discipline. Do you ever casually dismiss an authentic hadith because it seems unlikely? How will you respond to such reports from now on, knowing that they carry weight in the sight of Allah?
